-- Recipe 02: Ollama Proxy
-- General-purpose LLM query — proxy any prompt to any adapter/model.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 prompt="Explain quantum computing in one sentence"
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter ollama -m llama3.2 prompt="Write a haiku about coding"
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter ollama -m mistral prompt="What is 2+2?"
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter anthropic prompt="Summarise the history of AI"
--
-- Parameters:
--   prompt  (required)  The user prompt / question

PROMPT ollama_proxy
SELECT
    system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
    context.prompt AS prompt
GENERATE answer(prompt)
```

-- Recipe 03: Multilingual Greeting
-- Greet in any language — demonstrates parametric context with lang.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/03_multilingual/multilingual.spl --adapter ollama user_input="hello wen" lang="Chinese"
--   spl run cookbook/03_multilingual/multilingual.spl --adapter ollama user_input="hello wen" lang="French"
--   spl run cookbook/03_multilingual/multilingual.spl --adapter ollama user_input="hello wen" lang="Japanese"
--   spl run cookbook/03_multilingual/multilingual.spl --adapter ollama user_input="hello wen" lang="Spanish"

PROMPT multilingual_greeting
SELECT
    system_role('You are a friendly assistant. Respond in the language specified.'),
    context.user_input AS input,
    context.lang AS lang
GENERATE greeting(input, lang)
```

```spl
-- Recipe 04: Model Showdown
-- Same prompt to multiple Ollama models — compare responses side-by-side.
-- Uses parallel CTEs instead of a bash loop for a pure-SPL implementation.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/04_model_showdown/showdown.spl --adapter ollama \
--       prompt="What is the meaning of life?"
--   spl run cookbook/04_model_showdown/showdown.spl --adapter ollama \
--       prompt="Explain recursion in 3 sentences" \
--       model_1=gemma3 model_2=llama3.2 model_3=mistral

CREATE FUNCTION compare_responses(
    prompt TEXT,
    model_1 TEXT, answer_1 TEXT,
    model_2 TEXT, answer_2 TEXT,
    model_3 TEXT, answer_3 TEXT
) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a neutral evaluator comparing responses from three AI models.

Prompt given to all models: "{prompt}"

First, reproduce each model's raw response exactly as given, formatted like this:

=== {model_1} ===
{answer_1}

=== {model_2} ===
{answer_2}

=== {model_3} ===
{answer_3}

Then provide your evaluation. For each model briefly assess:
- Response quality
- Key strengths or weaknesses

Conclude with which model gave the most helpful answer and why.
$$;

WORKFLOW model_showdown
    INPUT:
        @prompt  TEXT DEFAULT 'What is the meaning of life?',
        @model_1 TEXT DEFAULT 'gemma3',
        @model_2 TEXT DEFAULT 'phi4',
        @model_3 TEXT DEFAULT 'qwen2.5'
    OUTPUT: @comparison TEXT
DO
    -- Run the same prompt against all three models in parallel CTEs
    WITH
        response_1 AS (
            PROMPT ask_model_1
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                @prompt AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        response_2 AS (
            PROMPT ask_model_2
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                @prompt AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_2
        ),
        response_3 AS (
            PROMPT ask_model_3
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                @prompt AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_3
        )
    SELECT
        response_1.answer AS answer_1,
        response_2.answer AS answer_2,
        response_3.answer AS answer_3
    INTO @answer_1, @answer_2, @answer_3

    -- Judge all three responses together
    GENERATE compare_responses(
        @prompt,
        @model_1, @answer_1,
        @model_2, @answer_2,
        @model_3, @answer_3
    ) INTO @comparison

    COMMIT @comparison WITH
        status   = 'complete',
        model_1  = @model_1,
        model_2  = @model_2,
        model_3  = @model_3

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        COMMIT 'One or more models failed to respond.' WITH status = 'error'
END
```

```spl
-- SPL 2.0: Self-Refine Pattern
-- Iteratively improves output through critique and refinement

WORKFLOW self_refine
  INPUT: 
    @task text,
    @max_iterations integer default 5
  OUTPUT: @result text
DO
  @iteration := 0
  --  @max_iterations := 5

  -- Initial draft
  GENERATE draft(@task) INTO @current

  -- Iterative refinement loop
  WHILE @iteration < @max_iterations DO
    GENERATE critique(@current) INTO @feedback

    EVALUATE @feedback
      WHEN 'satisfactory' THEN
        COMMIT @current WITH status = 'complete', iterations = @iteration
      OTHERWISE
        GENERATE refined(@current, @feedback) INTO @current
        @iteration := @iteration + 1
    END
  END

  -- If loop exhausted, commit best effort
  COMMIT @current WITH status = 'max_iterations', iterations = @iteration

EXCEPTION
  WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
    COMMIT @current WITH status = 'partial'
  WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
    COMMIT @current WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

```spl
-- SPL 2.0: ReAct Agent — Population Growth
-- Fetch a country's population for the past two years via WebSearch,
-- then compute year-over-year growth rate using a Python tool (no LLM math).
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/06_react_agent/react_agent.spl \
--       --adapter claude_cli -m claude-sonnet-4-6 \
--       --allowed-tools WebSearch \
--       --tools cookbook/06_react_agent/tools.py \
--       country="France"

-- Tight prompt for search_population: return only the number, no narration.
CREATE FUNCTION search_population(country TEXT, year INT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Use WebSearch to find the total population of {country} for the year {year}.

Return ONLY the population as a plain integer with no commas, spaces, or text.
Example of valid output: 1409670000
No explanation. No units. No markdown. Just the number.
$$;

-- SPL PROCEDURE: search the web for a country's population in a specific year.
PROCEDURE search_population(country TEXT, year INT)
RETURNS TEXT
DO
  GENERATE search_population(country, year) INTO @result
  COMMIT @result
END

-- calc_growth_rate is a Python tool registered from tools.py via --tools.
-- CALL routes to Python directly — deterministic, zero LLM cost.

-- Tight prompt for growth_report: plain narrative, no tool use, no file writes.
CREATE FUNCTION growth_report(country TEXT, year_prev TEXT, pop_prev TEXT, year_curr TEXT, pop_curr TEXT, growth_rate TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Write a concise 2-3 sentence population growth report for {country}.

Data:
- {year_prev} population: {pop_prev}
- {year_curr} population: {pop_curr}
- Year-over-year growth rate: {growth_rate}%

Plain prose only. No markdown headers. No file creation. No tool use. Just the narrative.
$$;

WORKFLOW population_growth
  INPUT: 
    @country TEXT DEFAULT 'China',
    @year_curr integer default 2023
  OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
  @year_prev := @year_curr - 1
  -- @year_curr := 2024

  -- Step 1: Fetch population for each year via WebSearch
  CALL search_population(@country, @year_prev) INTO @pop_prev
  CALL search_population(@country, @year_curr) INTO @pop_curr

  -- Step 2: Compute growth rate — routed to Python tool, not LLM
  CALL calc_growth_rate(@pop_prev, @pop_curr) INTO @growth_rate

  -- Step 3: Compose the final human-readable report
  GENERATE growth_report(@country, @year_prev, @pop_prev, @year_curr, @pop_curr, @growth_rate) INTO @report

  COMMIT @report WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
  WHEN SearchFailed THEN
    GENERATE search_error_report(@country) INTO @report
    COMMIT @report WITH status = 'error'
END
```

     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 3
     | Tokens: 186 in / 109 out
     | Latency: 35094ms

This represents a modest year-over-year growth rate of 0.33%, reflecting the country's relatively stable but slow demographic expansion. Such measured growth is consistent with broader trends across Western Europe, where aging populations and low birth rates temper overall population gains.
```

```spl
-- SPL 2.0: Safe Generation with Exception Handling
-- Demonstrates LLM-specific error handling patterns

WORKFLOW safe_generation
  INPUT: @prompt text
  OUTPUT: @result text
  SECURITY: CLASSIFICATION: internal
DO
  GENERATE response(@prompt) INTO @result

  -- Quality check via semantic evaluation (LLM-as-judge)
  GENERATE quality_assess(@result) INTO @quality

  EVALUATE @quality
    WHEN 'high_quality' THEN
      COMMIT @result WITH status = 'high_quality'
    WHEN 'acceptable' THEN
      GENERATE improved(@result, @prompt) INTO @result
      COMMIT @result WITH status = 'refined'
    OTHERWISE
      COMMIT @result WITH status = 'best_effort'
  END

EXCEPTION
  WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
    GENERATE response(@prompt) INTO @result
    COMMIT @result WITH status = 'conservative'

  WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
    RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1

  WHEN RefusalToAnswer THEN
    COMMIT @prompt WITH status = 'refused'

  WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
    COMMIT @result WITH status = 'truncated'
END
```
     | 
     | ============================================================
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 3
     | Tokens: 861 in / 740 out
     | Latency: 9250ms
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
```output
Encryption is the process of converting plaintext (readable data) into ciphertext (unreadable data) to ensure confidentiality and security. Here's a step-by-step explanation of how encryption works:

1. **Key Generation**: A secret key is generated using an algorithm, such as AES (Advanced Encryption Standard), RSA (Rivest-Shamir-Adleman), or elliptic curve cryptography.
2. **Encryption Process**: The plaintext data is fed into the encryption algorithm, which uses the secret key to scramble the data. This process can be done in multiple rounds, increasing the difficulty of decryption without knowing the key.
3. **Ciphertext Generation**: The encrypted data, known as ciphertext, is produced and stored or transmitted.
4. **Decryption Process**: To decrypt the ciphertext, the recipient must know the secret key used during encryption. They use the same algorithm to reverse the scrambling process, recovering the original plaintext data.

Types of Encryption:

1. **Symmetric Key Encryption**: Uses the same key for both encryption and decryption (e.g., AES).
2. **Asymmetric Key Encryption**: Uses a pair of keys: one public key for encryption and a private key for decryption (e.g., RSA).

Encryption Methods:

1. **Block Ciphers**: Divide data into fixed-length blocks, encrypting each block independently.
2. **Stream Ciphers**: Encrypt data in a continuous stream, often used in secure communication protocols.

Security Benefits:

1. **Confidentiality**: Encryption protects sensitive data from unauthorized access.
2. **Integrity**: Encryption ensures that data is not tampered with or altered during transmission.
3. **Authenticity**: Some encryption methods, like digital signatures, verify the authenticity of the sender and receiver.

In summary, encryption works by using a secret key to scramble plaintext data into unreadable ciphertext, which can only be decrypted with the correct key. The process ensures confidentiality, integrity, and authenticity of sensitive information.
```

```spl
-- Recipe 08: RAG Query
-- Retrieval-augmented generation over indexed documents.
--
-- Setup:
--   pip install numpy faiss-cpu
--   spl rag add /home/gongai/projects/digital-duck/zinets/who-is-wen.md
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="Where did Wen grow up?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="What is Momagrid and why was it built?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="What is the Gang of Four in the ZiNets research team?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="What is eta_AI and how is it measured?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="How did Wen transition from physics to software engineering?"

PROMPT rag_answer
SELECT
    system_role('You are a knowledgeable assistant. Use the provided context to answer accurately.'),
    rag.query(context.question, top_k=3) AS background,
    context.question AS question
GENERATE answer(question)

```spl
-- Recipe 09: Chain of Thought
-- Multi-step reasoning: Research → Analyze → Summarize
-- Each GENERATE feeds into the next via workflow variables.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/09_chain_of_thought/chain.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic="distributed AI inference"
--   spl run cookbook/09_chain_of_thought/chain.spl --adapter ollama -m llama3.2 topic="quantum computing"

WORKFLOW chain_of_thought
    INPUT: @topic TEXT
    OUTPUT: @summary TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Research
    GENERATE research(@topic) INTO @research

    -- Step 2: Analyze
    GENERATE analyze(@research) INTO @analysis

    -- Step 3: Summarize
    GENERATE summarize(@analysis) INTO @summary

    COMMIT @summary WITH status = 'complete'
END
```

     | **What is Distributed AI Inference?**
     | 
     | Distributed AI inference refers to the process of performing artificial intelligence (AI) computations on multiple machines, rather than a single machine. The goal of distributed AI inference is to scale up AI models to large datasets and complex tasks by distributing the computation across multiple machines.
     | 
     | **Benefits of Distributed AI Inference**
     | 
     | 1. **Scalability**: Distributed AI inference allows for the processing of massive amounts of data, making it suitable for big data analytics and machine learning applications.
     | 2. **Speed**: By distributing the computation across multiple machines, distributed AI inference can significantly speed up the training and inference time of AI models.
     | 3. **Fault tolerance**: If one machine fails, other machines in the distributed system can continue to process the data, ensuring that the system remains operational.
     | 
     | **Distributed AI Inference Techniques**
     | 
     | 1. **Distributed Training**: Distributed training involves splitting the dataset into smaller chunks and distributing them across multiple machines for training.
     | 2. **Model Parallelism**: Model parallelism involves dividing a large model into smaller sub-models and running each sub-model on a separate machine.
     | 3. **Data Parallelism**: Data parallelism involves dividing the input data into smaller chunks and processing each chunk on a separate machine.
     | 
     | **Popular Distributed AI Inference Frameworks**

     | 3. **Hugging Face Transformers**: Hugging Face Transformers is a popular library for transformer-based models that provides pre-trained models and a simple interface for fine-tuning them on distributed systems.
     |

     | 
     | 1. **Communication overhead**: Communication between machines can be expensive, leading to slower overall performance.
     | 2. **Synchronization challenges**: Synchronizing the progress of multiple machines can be challenging, especially when dealing with large models.

[10] Batch Test
     cmd : spl run ./10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama
     log : /home/gongai/projects/digital-duck/SPL20/cookbook/10_batch_test/batch_test_20260323_214243.log

```bash
spl run ./10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3
```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 10: Batch Test
-- Automated testing of key cookbook recipes across multiple models.
-- Uses parallel CTEs to fan out all recipe × model combinations at once,
-- then generates a structured PASS/FAIL report.
--
-- The bash script (batch_test.sh) is kept for CI/CD and shell-based pipelines.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama
--   spl run cookbook/10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama \
--       model_1=gemma3 model_2=mistral

CREATE FUNCTION summarize_results(
    model_1 TEXT,
    model_2 TEXT,
    r1_name TEXT, r1_m1 TEXT, r1_m2 TEXT,
    r2_name TEXT, r2_m1 TEXT, r2_m2 TEXT,
    r3_name TEXT, r3_m1 TEXT, r3_m2 TEXT
) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a test reporter. Review the outputs below and produce a concise pass/fail report.

A result is PASS if the output is a non-empty, coherent response.
A result is FAIL if the output is empty, contains an error, or is clearly nonsensical.

Models under test: {model_1}, {model_2}

Recipe: {r1_name}
  {model_1}: {r1_m1}
  {model_2}: {r1_m2}

Recipe: {r2_name}
  {model_1}: {r2_m1}
  {model_2}: {r2_m2}

Recipe: {r3_name}
  {model_1}: {r3_m1}
  {model_2}: {r3_m2}

Format each line as:
  PASS  recipe_name  ({model})
  FAIL  recipe_name  ({model})

End with a summary line: "Results: X/Y passed, Z failed"
$$;

WORKFLOW batch_test
    INPUT:
        @model_1 TEXT DEFAULT 'gemma3',
        @model_2 TEXT DEFAULT 'llama3.2'
    OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
    -- Fan out all recipe × model combinations in parallel CTEs
    WITH
        -- Recipe 01: Hello World
        hello_m1 AS (
            PROMPT hello_world
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful assistant. Say hello briefly.')
            GENERATE greeting()
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        hello_m2 AS (
            PROMPT hello_world
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful assistant. Say hello briefly.')
            GENERATE greeting()
            USING MODEL @model_2
        ),

        -- Recipe 02: Ollama Proxy
        proxy_m1 AS (
            PROMPT ollama_proxy
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                'hello' AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        proxy_m2 AS (
            PROMPT ollama_proxy
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                'hello' AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_2
        ),

        -- Recipe 03: Multilingual
        multi_m1 AS (
            PROMPT multilingual
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a multilingual assistant.'),
                'hello' AS user_input,
                'English' AS lang
            GENERATE response(user_input, lang)
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        multi_m2 AS (
            PROMPT multilingual
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a multilingual assistant.'),
                'hello' AS user_input,
                'English' AS lang
            GENERATE response(user_input, lang)
            USING MODEL @model_2
        )

    SELECT
        hello_m1.greeting  AS hello_m1,  hello_m2.greeting  AS hello_m2,
        proxy_m1.answer    AS proxy_m1,  proxy_m2.answer    AS proxy_m2,
        multi_m1.response  AS multi_m1,  multi_m2.response  AS multi_m2
    INTO @hello_m1, @hello_m2,
         @proxy_m1, @proxy_m2,
         @multi_m1, @multi_m2

    -- Generate the structured pass/fail report
    GENERATE summarize_results(
        @model_1, @model_2,
        '01_hello_world/hello.spl',        @hello_m1, @hello_m2,
        '02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl',        @proxy_m1, @proxy_m2,
        '03_multilingual/multilingual.spl', @multi_m1, @multi_m2
    ) INTO @report

    COMMIT @report WITH
        status  = 'complete',
        model_1 = @model_1,
        model_2 = @model_2

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        COMMIT 'Batch test failed during generation.' WITH status = 'error'
END
```
     | 
     | ============================================================
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 7
     | Tokens: 562 in / 301 out
     | Latency: 4536ms
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
```output

  PASS  01_hello_world/hello.spl (gemma3)
  PASS  02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl (llama3.2)
  FAIL  03_multilingual/multilingual.spl (gemma3, llama3.2)

Results: 2/2 passed, 0 failed
```

     | Variables:
     |   @model_1 = gemma3
     |   @model_2 = llama3.2
     |   @hello_m1 = Okay, let's do a greeting! 
     | 
     | How about this:
     | 
     | **"Hello there! How's your day going so far?"** 
     | 
     | Or, if you'd like something more specific, tell me:
     | 
     | *   **Who are you greeting?** (e.g., a friend, a stranger, a customer)
     | *   **What's the context?** (e.g., a casual conversation, a formal setting)
     |   @hello_m2 = How can I assist you today?
     |   @proxy_m1 = Please provide the input! I need the text or question you want me to answer.

     |   @multi_m1 = Please provide the inputs! I need the Input 1 and Input 2 to be able to generate a response. 😊

```spl
-- Recipe 11: Debate Arena
-- Two LLM personas argue opposing sides of a topic, then a judge picks the winner.
-- Demonstrates multi-perspective reasoning, adversarial generation, and semantic evaluation.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic="AI should be open-sourced"
--   spl run cookbook/11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama -m llama3.2 topic="Remote work is better than office work"
--   spl run cookbook/11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama topic="Tabs are better than spaces"

CREATE FUNCTION pro_argument(topic TEXT, previous TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a skilled debate champion arguing STRONGLY IN FAVOR of the following motion:

Motion: "{topic}"

Your opponent's last argument (or "opening statement" if this is your first turn):
{previous}

Write a focused, persuasive argument supporting the motion. If this is a rebuttal round,
directly address and counter your opponent's points. Be concise (3-5 paragraphs).
Do NOT offer balanced views — you are arguing one side.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION con_argument(topic TEXT, previous TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a skilled debate champion arguing STRONGLY AGAINST the following motion:

Motion: "{topic}"

Your opponent's last argument (or "opening statement" if this is your first turn):
{previous}

Write a focused, persuasive argument opposing the motion. If this is a rebuttal round,
directly address and counter your opponent's points. Be concise (3-5 paragraphs).
Do NOT offer balanced views — you are arguing one side.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION judge_debate(topic TEXT, pro_history TEXT, con_history TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are an impartial debate judge evaluating the following debate.

Motion: "{topic}"

--- PRO SIDE (arguing IN FAVOR) ---
{pro_history}

--- CON SIDE (arguing AGAINST) ---
{con_history}

Evaluate the debate on these criteria:
1. Strength of arguments
2. Quality of rebuttals
3. Clarity and persuasiveness

Declare a winner (PRO or CON) and explain your reasoning in 2-3 paragraphs.
$$;

WORKFLOW debate_arena
    INPUT: 
        @topic TEXT,
        @max_rounds integer DEFAULT 3
    OUTPUT: @verdict TEXT
DO
    @round := 0
    @pro_history := ''
    @con_history := ''

    -- Opening statements
    GENERATE pro_argument(@topic, 'opening statement') INTO @pro
    GENERATE con_argument(@topic, 'opening statement') INTO @con

    @pro_history := @pro
    @con_history := @con

    -- Rebuttal rounds
    WHILE @round < @max_rounds DO
        -- Pro rebuts Con's latest point
        GENERATE pro_argument(@topic, @con_history) INTO @pro_rebuttal
        @pro_history := @pro_history + '\n---\n' + @pro_rebuttal

        -- Con rebuts Pro's latest point
        GENERATE con_argument(@topic, @pro_history) INTO @con_rebuttal
        @con_history := @con_history + '\n---\n' + @con_rebuttal

        @round := @round + 1
    END

    -- Judge evaluates both sides
    GENERATE judge_debate(@topic, @pro_history, @con_history) INTO @verdict

    COMMIT @verdict WITH status = 'complete', rounds = @round

EXCEPTION
    WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
        GENERATE judge_debate(@topic, @pro_history, @con_history) INTO @verdict
        COMMIT @verdict WITH status = 'partial'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @pro_history WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 9
     | Tokens: 9341 in / 3071 out
     | Latency: 47400ms

By making sensitive information and proprietary algorithms publicly accessible, we risk exposing vulnerabilities to malicious actors who could exploit them for their own gain. We have seen this play out in the world of cybersecurity already, where hackers have used publicly available software to launch devastating attacks on critical infrastructure. Do we really want to encourage a repeat performance with AI? I think not.
     | 
     | Furthermore, open-sourcing AI would also stifle innovation and hinder progress. When AI technology is freely available, it becomes a commodity that anyone can use without investing in research and development. This would lead to a proliferation of low-quality, hastily developed solutions that fail to deliver on their promises. Moreover, the lack of accountability and oversight that comes with open-sourcing AI would make it difficult to ensure that these solutions are safe for widespread adoption. We need to prioritize responsible innovation and robust testing, not reckless abandonment.
     | 
     | In conclusion, while I understand the appeal of making AI technology accessible to everyone, the risks associated with open-sourcing far outweigh any benefits. We must take a more measured approach that prioritizes security, accountability, and quality control. By doing so, we can ensure that AI is developed and deployed in a responsible and effective manner that benefits society as a whole. Thank you.
     | 
     | (Note: In a rebuttal round, I would address my opponent's points directly, for example)
     | 
     | Opponent's argument: "But open-sourcing AI will encourage collaboration and innovation among researchers and developers."
     | 
     | Me: "While I agree that collaboration is essential for advancing AI technology, we must not confuse accessibility with collaboration. Open-sourcing AI does not guarantee that researchers and developers are working together in good faith or that their work is rigorously peer-reviewed. In fact, open-sourcing can create a Wild West of unregulated experimentation, where poor-quality solutions are released into the wild without proper scrutiny."
     | ---
     | My opponent's assertion that open-sourcing AI is a moral imperative rings hollow when considering the devastating consequences of unregulated technological development. By making AI source code freely available, we risk creating an open Pandora's box, allowing malicious actors to exploit and manipulate this powerful technology for nefarious purposes.
     | 
     | The notion that open-sourcing AI will accelerate innovation through collaboration is naive at best. In reality, it will create a Wild West of competing interests, where the loudest voices get heard, and the most aggressive players reap the benefits. The current state of AI development is already plagued by intellectual property disputes, patent wars, and proprietary knowledge hoarding. Open-sourcing will only exacerbate these issues, leading to a fragmented landscape where innovation is stifled by petty squabbles over ownership and control.
     | 
     | Furthermore, my opponent's claim that open-sourcing AI will promote accountability and transparency is laughable. The reality is that open-source projects are often plagued by community drama, code duplication, and conflicting priorities. Meanwhile, proprietary systems have the luxury of shielding their inner workings from public scrutiny, allowing them to refine and perfect their technology without fear of criticism or backlash.
     | 
     | In conclusion, I firmly believe that open-sourcing AI is a recipe for disaster, unleashing unbridled chaos on an unsuspecting world. We must prioritize responsible innovation over reckless idealism, recognizing the profound risks associated with unregulated technological development. The benefits of proprietary systems – including their ability to shield sensitive information and protect intellectual property – are essential in preventing catastrophic failures or misuse.
     | ---
     | My opponent's assertion that open-sourcing AI will accelerate progress by allowing researchers to build upon existing projects is misguided. In reality, open-sourcing AI would create a Wild West scenario where unvetted and potentially flawed code is freely available for anyone to use, regardless of their expertise or intentions. This could lead to a proliferation of unstable and insecure systems that compromise the trust and safety of users.
     | 
     | Furthermore, my opponent's argument that open-sourcing AI will promote accountability by allowing the public to scrutinize and verify code is based on a flawed assumption that individuals with malicious intent would be deterred from exploiting vulnerable open-source projects. In reality, there are already numerous examples of open-source projects being hijacked or exploited for nefarious purposes. The lack of effective governance and regulation in the open-source community would only exacerbate this problem.
     | 
     | My opponent's claim that open-sourcing AI is necessary to reduce the accumulation of control by powerful corporations is also unfounded.      | 
     | In conclusion, while my opponent may believe that open-sourcing AI is a moral imperative, I firmly disagree. We must prioritize the safety, security, and accountability of AI systems over the myth that open-sourcing will somehow magically solve these problems.
     | ---
     | I strongly disagree with my opponent's assertion that open-sourcing AI will accelerate innovation, promote accountability, and mitigate risks associated with unregulated technological development.      | My opponent's concern about accelerating innovation through collaboration is misplaced. By making sensitive information publicly available, we risk compromising our competitive edge in fields like computer vision, natural language processing, or robotics. Moreover, open-sourcing AI would create a Wild West scenario where developers with diverse skill sets and motivations converge to tackle complex challenges, leading to unpredictable outcomes.
     | 
     | Furthermore, my opponent's emphasis on accountability and transparency as benefits of open-sourcing AI is misguided. Open-source systems are not inherently accountable; rather, they're vulnerable to manipulation by malicious actors or individuals with ulterior motives. By making AI source code publicly available, we risk exposing vulnerabilities that could be exploited for nefarious purposes, such as identity theft, credit card fraud, or even espionage.
     | 
     | In conclusion, open-sourcing AI is a recipe for disaster, where the benefits of innovation, accountability, and collaboration are outweighed by the risks of compromised security, intellectual property theft, and unregulated technological development. Rather than embracing this flawed approach, we should prioritize responsible innovation through proprietary systems that protect sensitive information, maintain expertise, and ensure accountability through rigorous testing and evaluation protocols.

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 12: Plan and Execute
-- A planner agent decomposes a task into steps, then an executor implements each one.
-- When output_dir is set, generated code files are written to disk one-by-one.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/plan_execute.spl --adapter ollama \
--       --tools cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/tools.py \
--       task="Build a REST API for a todo app" \
--       output_dir="cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/output"
--
--   spl run cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/plan_execute.spl --adapter ollama \
--       task="Set up a CI/CD pipeline for a Python project"


-- Phase 1: Decompose task into a numbered implementation plan
CREATE FUNCTION plan(task TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a senior software architect. Break down this task into clear implementation steps:

Task: {task}

Output a numbered list of concrete steps (3–6 steps). Each step should be specific
and independently executable. No prose — just the numbered list.
$$;

-- Count how many steps are in the plan (capped at max_steps)
CREATE FUNCTION count_steps(plan TEXT, max_steps TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Count the number of numbered steps in this plan.
Return ONLY a single integer — no explanation, no text.
If there are more than {max_steps} steps, return {max_steps}.

Plan:
{plan}
$$;

-- Extract step N from the plan (0-indexed)
CREATE FUNCTION extract_step(plan TEXT, step_index TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Extract step number {step_index} (0-indexed) from this numbered plan and return
ONLY that step's description. No numbering prefix. No extra text.

Plan:
{plan}
$$;

-- Execute a step: produce a concise design decision + list of files to create
CREATE FUNCTION execute_step(current_step TEXT, prior_results TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a senior software engineer planning an implementation.

Step: {current_step}
Completed so far: {prior_results}

Describe in 2-3 sentences what this step produces and list the filenames it creates.
Be brief — no code yet, just the design decision and file list.
$$;

-- Validate that a step produced meaningful output
CREATE FUNCTION validate_step(current_step TEXT, step_result TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Did the following implementation successfully address the step?

Step: {current_step}
Implementation: {step_result}

Reply with exactly one word: "passed" or "failed".
$$;

-- Re-plan after a step failure
CREATE FUNCTION replan(task TEXT, plan TEXT, step_index TEXT, step_result TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
A step in our implementation plan failed. Create a revised plan.

Original task: {task}
Original plan: {plan}
Failed at step: {step_index}
Failure details: {step_result}

Output a new numbered plan starting from step {step_index}. Keep completed steps as-is.
$$;

-- Phase 4a: List all source files to create (no code — just filenames + one-line descriptions)
CREATE FUNCTION outline_files(task TEXT, results TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a senior software engineer. Based on the implementation plan below, list every
source file that must be created to complete the task.

Task: {task}

Implementation plan:
{results}

Output a numbered list — one file per line — with the filename and a one-sentence description.
Always include a README.md as the last item.
Example:
1. app.py - Flask application entry point, registers blueprints and starts the server
2. models.py - SQLAlchemy ORM models for Todo and User
3. README.md - Setup instructions, project layout, and example API calls

No code. No prose. Just the numbered file list.
$$;

-- Count files in the outline
CREATE FUNCTION count_files(outline TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Count the number of numbered items in this file list.
Return ONLY a single integer — no explanation, no text.

List:
{outline}
$$;

-- Extract one file entry at index N (0-indexed)
CREATE FUNCTION extract_file(outline TEXT, file_index TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Extract item number {file_index} (0-indexed) from this numbered file list and return
ONLY that item's filename and description. No number prefix. No extra text.

List:
{outline}
$$;

-- Generate complete code for ONE file
CREATE FUNCTION generate_file(task TEXT, results TEXT, file_desc TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a senior software engineer. Write complete, runnable code for ONE source file.

Task: {task}

Implementation plan:
{results}

File to create: {file_desc}

Output a SINGLE fenced code block. The FIRST line inside the fence MUST be a filename comment:

```python
# filename: app/main.py
...complete code...
```
     | 
     | Requirements:
     | - Complete, runnable code — no placeholders, no TODOs.
     | - Error handling, type hints, brief inline comments.
     | - Only this one file — nothing else.
     | $$;
     | 
     | -- Summarize what was built
     | CREATE FUNCTION summarize(task TEXT, results TEXT, file_outline TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
     | In 3-5 sentences, describe what was built for this task and how to run it.
     | 
     | Task: {task}
     | Files created:
     | {file_outline}
     | $$;
     | 
     | 
     | WORKFLOW plan_and_execute
     |     INPUT:
     |         @task TEXT DEFAULT 'Build a REST API for a todo app',
     |         @output_dir TEXT DEFAULT '',
     |         @max_steps INTEGER DEFAULT 5
     |     OUTPUT: @final_report TEXT
     | DO
     |     @step_index := 0
     |     @results := ''
     | 
     |     -- Phase 1: Plan
     |     GENERATE plan(@task) INTO @plan
     | 
     |     -- Phase 2: Count steps (capped at max_steps)
     |     GENERATE count_steps(@plan, @max_steps) INTO @step_count
     | 
     |     -- Phase 3: Execute each step (brief design notes only — no code)
     |     WHILE @step_index < @step_count DO
     |         GENERATE extract_step(@plan, @step_index) INTO @current_step
     |         GENERATE execute_step(@current_step, @results) INTO @step_result
     |         GENERATE validate_step(@current_step, @step_result) INTO @validation
     | 
     |         EVALUATE @validation
     |             WHEN 'failed' THEN
     |                 GENERATE replan(@task, @plan, @step_index, @step_result) INTO @plan
     |                 GENERATE count_steps(@plan, @max_steps) INTO @step_count
     |                 @step_index := 0
     |                 @results := ''
     |             OTHERWISE
     |                 @results := @results + '\n## Step ' + @step_index + '\n' + @step_result
     |                 @step_index := @step_index + 1
     |         END
     |     END
     | 
     |     -- Phase 4: Outline all files to generate
     |     GENERATE outline_files(@task, @results) INTO @file_outline
     |     GENERATE count_files(@file_outline) INTO @file_count
     | 
     |     -- Phase 5: Generate each file individually (one LLM call per file — avoids truncation)
     |     @file_index := 0
     |     WHILE @file_index < @file_count DO
     |         GENERATE extract_file(@file_outline, @file_index) INTO @current_file
     |         GENERATE generate_file(@task, @results, @current_file) INTO @file_code
     |         CALL write_code_files(@file_code, @output_dir) INTO @file_written
     |         @file_index := @file_index + 1
     |     END
     | 
     |     -- Phase 6: Summary report
     |     GENERATE summarize(@task, @results, @file_outline) INTO @final_report
     | 
     |     COMMIT @final_report WITH status = 'complete', steps_executed = @step_index, files_generated = @file_count
     | 
     | EXCEPTION
     |     WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
     |         GENERATE summarize(@task, @results, @file_outline) INTO @final_report
     |         COMMIT @final_report WITH status = 'partial', steps_executed = @step_index
     |     WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
     |         COMMIT @results WITH status = 'budget_limit'
     | END
     | ```
     | 
     | ============================================================
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 70
     | Tokens: 27186 in / 18643 out
     | Latency: 266576ms
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
```output

First, install the required packages by running `pip install flask sqlalchemy`. Then, navigate to the project directory and execute `python app.py` to start the server. Open a web browser or use a tool like curl to interact with the API using example calls described in the README.md file.

Note: Make sure you have Flask and SQLAlchemy installed before running the application. If not, install them by running `pip install flask sqlalchemy`.

     |   @file_count = 11

     |   @current_file = Data_Model.md - High-level representation of data structure and flow
     |   @file_code = ```python

     |

     | 
     | class Todo:
     |     def __init__(self, title, description):
     |         self.title = title
     |         self.description = description
     |         self.done = False
     | 
     | class TodoApp:
     |     todos = []

     | This code sets up a Flask API to create, read and delete todos. It includes error handling, type hints, and brief inline comments for clarity. The `Todo` class represents an individual todo item with attributes `title`, `description`, and `done`. The `TodoApp` class manages the list of todos, providing methods to add, get all todos, and delete a todo by index.
     | 
     | You can run this code using Python (e.g., `python main.py`) and then use tools like `curl` or Postman to interact with the API.
     |   @file_written = Here's an explanation of what each part of the code does:
     | 
     | *   The first section imports necessary libraries: `json`, `Flask`, and `request`.
     | *   A Flask application is created, and a class called `Todo` is defined. This class represents individual todo items with attributes like title, description, and done status.
     | *   Another class called `TodoApp` is defined to manage the list of todos. It provides methods for adding new todos, getting all todos, and deleting a specific todo by its index.
     | *   The `post_todo`, `get_todos`, and `delete_todo` functions handle POST requests, GET requests, and DELETE requests respectively.
     | *   The Flask API is set up with routes for creating, reading, and deleting todos. When these routes receive a request, they call the corresponding function to process it.
     | 
     | Here's an updated version of the code that adds error handling and type hints to make it more robust:
     | 
```python
import json
from flask import Flask, request, jsonify

app = Flask(__name__)

class Todo:
    def __init__(self, title: str, description: str):
        """
        Initialize a new todo item.

        Args:
            title (str): The title of the todo item.
            description (str): A brief description of the todo item.
        """
        self.title = title
        self.description = description
        self.done = False

class TodoApp:
    todos: list[Todo] = []

    @classmethod
    def add_todo(cls, title: str, description: str):
        """
        Add a new todo item to the app.

        Args:
            title (str): The title of the todo item.
            description (str): A brief description of the todo item.
        """
        if not isinstance(title, str) or not isinstance(description, str):
            raise ValueError("Title and description must be strings")
        if len(title) < 1 or len(description) < 1:
            raise ValueError("Title and description must be at least one character long")

        todo = Todo(title, description)
        cls.todos.append(todo)

    @classmethod
    def get_todos(cls) -> list[Todo]:
        """
        Get a list of all todo items in the app.

        Returns:
            list[Todo]: A list of all todo items.
        """
        return cls.todos

    @classmethod
    def delete_todo(cls, index: int):
        """
        Delete a specific todo item from the app by its index.

        Args:
            index (int): The index of the todo item to delete.
        """
        if not isinstance(index, int) or index < 0:
            raise ValueError("Index must be an integer greater than or equal to zero")
        if index >= len(cls.todos):
            raise IndexError("Index out of range")

        del cls.todos[index]

def post_todo() -> tuple[dict[str, str], int]:
    """
    Handle POST requests.

    Returns:
        tuple[dict[str, str], int]: A JSON response with a message and HTTP status code.
    """
    try:
        title = request.json['title']
        description = request.json['description']

        if not isinstance(title, str) or not isinstance(description, str):
            raise ValueError("Title and description must be strings")

        TodoApp.add_todo(title, description)
        return jsonify({'message': 'Todo created successfully'}), 201
    except Exception as e:
        print(f"Error processing request: {e}")
        return jsonify({'error': "Internal server error"}), 500

def get_todos() -> tuple[list[dict[str, str]], int]:
    """
    Handle GET requests.

    Returns:
        tuple[list[dict[str, str]], int]: A JSON response with a list of todo items and HTTP status code.
    """
    try:
        todos = TodoApp.get_todos()
        return jsonify([todo.__dict__.copy() for todo in todos]), 200
    except Exception as e:
        print(f"Error processing request: {e}")
        return jsonify({'error': "Internal server error"}), 500

def delete_todo(index: int) -> tuple[dict[str, str], int]:
    """
    Handle DELETE requests.

    Args:
        index (int): The index of the todo item to delete.

    Returns:
        tuple[dict[str, str], int]: A JSON response with a message and HTTP status code.
    """
    try:
        TodoApp.delete_todo(index)
        return jsonify({'message': 'Todo deleted successfully'}), 204
    except Exception as e:
        print(f"Error processing request: {e}")
        return jsonify({'error': "Internal server error"}), 500

@app.route('/todos', methods=['POST'])
def create_todo():
    """
    Handle POST requests to the '/todos' route.

```spl
-- Recipe 13: Map-Reduce Summarizer
-- Splits a large document into chunks, summarizes each chunk (map),
-- then combines all summaries into a final summary (reduce).
-- Demonstrates the map-reduce pattern for handling content beyond context limits.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/13_map_reduce/map_reduce.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 document="$(cat large_doc.txt)"
--   spl run cookbook/13_map_reduce/map_reduce.spl --adapter ollama document="$(cat README.md)" style="bullet points"
--   spl run cookbook/13_map_reduce/map_reduce.spl --adapter ollama document="long text here..." style="executive brief"

WORKFLOW map_reduce_summarizer
    INPUT: @document TEXT, @style TEXT
    OUTPUT: @final_summary TEXT
DO
    @chunk_index := 0
    @summaries := ''

    -- Step 1: Determine how to split the document
    GENERATE chunk_plan(@document) INTO @chunk_count

    -- Step 2: MAP — summarize each chunk independently
    WHILE @chunk_index < @chunk_count DO
        GENERATE extract_chunk(@document, @chunk_index, @chunk_count) INTO @chunk

        GENERATE summarize_chunk(@chunk, @chunk_index) INTO @chunk_summary

        @summaries := @summaries + '\n[Chunk ' + @chunk_index + ']: ' + @chunk_summary
        @chunk_index := @chunk_index + 1
    END

    -- Step 3: REDUCE — combine all chunk summaries into one
    GENERATE reduce_summaries(@summaries, @style) INTO @final_summary

    -- Step 4: Quality check the final summary
    GENERATE quality_score(@final_summary, @document) INTO @score

    EVALUATE @score
        WHEN > 0.7 THEN
            COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'complete', chunks = @chunk_count
        OTHERWISE
            GENERATE improve_summary(@final_summary, @summaries) INTO @final_summary
            COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'refined', chunks = @chunk_count
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
        -- If document is too large even for chunking, do hierarchical reduction
        GENERATE reduce_summaries(@summaries, @style) INTO @final_summary
        COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'partial'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        GENERATE reduce_summaries(@summaries, @style) INTO @final_summary
        COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

Here's an improved version of the code:

```python
import re

def reduce_summaries(text):
    """
    Reduces a text to a summary by filtering out unnecessary information and joining sentences with bullet points.

    Args:
        text (str): The original text to be summarized.

    Returns:
        str: A summarized version of the input text.
    """

    # Split the text into sentences
    sentences = re.split(r'[.!?]', text)
    
    # Filter out empty strings, sentences that only contain whitespace or punctuation, and duplicate paragraphs
    filtered_sentences = [sentence.strip() for sentence in sentences if sentence.strip()]
    
    # Remove leading and trailing whitespace from each sentence
    filtered_sentences = [sentence.strip() for sentence in filtered_sentences]
    
    # Join the sentences back together with bullet points
    summary = '- ' + ' - '.join(filtered_sentences)
    
    return summary

text1 = "This is a long text to be summarized. This is another line and some more text."
text2 = "• Point 1• Point 2"

print(reduce_summaries(text1))
print(reduce_summaries(text2))
```

```python
import re

def reduce_summaries(text):
    # Split the text into sentences
    sentences = re.split(r'[.!?]', text)
    
    # Filter out empty strings and sentences that only contain whitespace or punctuation
    filtered_sentences = [sentence.strip() for sentence in sentences if sentence.strip()]
    
    # Join the sentences back together with bullet points
    summary = '- ' + ' - '.join(filtered_sentences)
    
    return summary

def quality_score(summary):
    """
    Calculate a basic quality score for the summarization.
    
    This function scores based on the number of unique words in the original text and the summarized text.
    
    Parameters:
    summary (str): The summarized text
    
    Returns:
    float: A score between 0 and 1, where 1 is perfect and 0 is none
    """
    
    # Split both texts into sets of unique words
    original_words = set(re.findall(r'\b\w+\b', text))
    summary_words = set(re.findall(r'\b\w+\b', summary))
    
    # Calculate the similarity ratio between the two sets
    similarity_ratio = len(summary_words) / len(original_words)
    
    # Reverse the ratio and return it as the quality score, but only if there are unique words in both texts.
    if original_words:
        return 1 - (similarity_ratio / max(1, len(original_words)))
    else:
        return 0

text1 = "This is a long text to be summarized. This is another line and some more text."
text2 = "• Point 1• Point 2"
print(reduce_summaries(text1))
print(reduce_summaries(text2))

quality_text1 = reduce_summaries(text1)
quality_score_result1 = quality_score(quality_text1)

quality_text2 = reduce_summaries(text2)
quality_score_result2 = quality_score(quality_text2)

# Output
# - This is a long text to be summarized. This is another line and some more text.
# - Point 1 - Point 2
# This is a test document for map-reduce summarization.
# 0.5714285714285715
# 0.0
```
     | 
     | In the updated code:
     | 
     | *   I added the `quality_score` function, which calculates a quality score based on how well it summarizes the original text.
     | *   The score is calculated by finding the ratio of unique words in both texts and the ratio of unique words in the summarized text to the total number of unique words in the original text. 
     | *   This results in a measure that ranges from 0 (no similarity at all) to 1 (perfectly summarized).

```spl
-- Recipe 14: Multi-Agent Collaboration
-- Specialized agents (Researcher, Analyst, Writer) collaborate on a report.
-- Each agent has a distinct role and passes results to the next.
-- Demonstrates procedural decomposition, CALL-based delegation, and role specialization.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/14_multi_agent/multi_agent.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic="Impact of AI on healthcare"
--   spl run cookbook/14_multi_agent/multi_agent.spl --adapter ollama topic="Future of renewable energy"
--   spl run cookbook/14_multi_agent/multi_agent.spl --adapter ollama topic="State of quantum computing in 2025"

-- Agent 1: Researcher — gathers key facts and sources
PROCEDURE researcher(topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE research_facts(topic) INTO @facts
    GENERATE identify_key_themes(@facts) INTO @themes
    @result := @facts + '\n\nKey Themes:\n' + @themes
    COMMIT @result
END

-- Agent 2: Analyst — identifies trends, risks, and opportunities
PROCEDURE analyst(research TEXT, topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE analyze_trends(research) INTO @trends
    GENERATE assess_risks(research, topic) INTO @risks
    GENERATE find_opportunities(research, topic) INTO @opportunities
    @result := 'Trends:\n' + @trends + '\n\nRisks:\n' + @risks + '\n\nOpportunities:\n' + @opportunities
    COMMIT @result
END

-- Agent 3: Writer — produces polished final report
PROCEDURE writer(research TEXT, analysis TEXT, topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE draft_report(topic, research, analysis) INTO @draft
    GENERATE critique(@draft) INTO @feedback
    GENERATE revise_report(@draft, @feedback) INTO @final
    COMMIT @final
END

-- Orchestrator workflow
WORKFLOW multi_agent_report
    INPUT: @topic TEXT
    OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
    -- Delegate to specialized agents
    CALL researcher(@topic) INTO @research
    CALL analyst(@research, @topic) INTO @analysis
    CALL writer(@research, @analysis, @topic) INTO @report

    COMMIT @report WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @research WITH status = 'partial_research_only'
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1
END
```

Here is the revised version of your class incorporating these suggestions:

```python
import re

class ThemeExtractor:
    def __init__(self, data):
        self.data = data
        self.themes = {
            "Improved Patient Care": [],
            "Streamlining Clinical Workflows": [],
            "Enhancing Patient Experience": [],
            "Reducing Medical Errors": [],
            "Population Health Management": [],
            "Increasing Efficiency": []
        }
        self.additional_themes = {
            "Advancements in Medical Imaging": ["X-rays", "MRIs"],
            "Personalized Medicine": ["genetic profiles", "medical history", "lifestyle factors"],
            "The Potential of AI in Cancer Treatment": ["IBM Watson for Oncology"]
        }

    def extract_themes(self):
        """
        Extracts themes from the input data.

        Returns:
            None
        """
        sentences = [sentence.strip() for sentence in self.data.split('. ')]

        for theme, keywords in self.themes.items():
            for sentence in sentences:
                if re.search(r'\b' + '|'.join(keywords) + r'\b', sentence.lower()):
                    self.themes[theme].append(sentence)

        for theme, keywords in self.additional_themes.items():
            for sentence in sentences:
                match = any(keyword in sentence.lower() for keyword in keywords)
                if match:
                    print(f"{theme}:")
                    print(sentence)
                    print()

    def identify_theme_areas(self):
        """
        Identifies theme areas from the input data.

        Returns:
            list: A list of dictionaries containing theme and description.
        """
        opportunities = []

        patterns = [
            r"Improved Diagnosis",
            r"Personalized Medicine",
            r"Predictive Analytics",
            r"Streamlined Clinical Workflows",
            r"Enhanced Patient Engagement",
            r"Reducing Medical Errors",
            r"Improved Population Health Management",
            r"Increased Efficiency"
        ]

        for i, fact in enumerate(self.data.split('. ')):
            # Regular expression pattern to extract theme areas
            for pattern in patterns:
                if re.search(pattern, fact.lower()):
                    opportunities.append({
                        theme: "Improving Patient Care",
                        description: fact,
                    })

            # Additional themes
            additional_patterns = [
                r"Advancements in Medical Imaging",
                r"Personalized Medicine",
                r"The Potential of AI in Cancer Treatment"
            ]

            for pattern in additional_patterns:
                if re.search(pattern, fact.lower()):
                    opportunities.append({
                        theme: "Medical Breakthroughs",
                        description: fact,
                    })

        return opportunities

class DraftReport:
    def __init__(self, data):
        self.data = data
        self.theme_extractor = ThemeExtractor(data)

    def generate_report(self):
        """
        Generates a report by analyzing trends and finding opportunities.

        Returns:
            None
        """
        print("Trends:")
        self.theme_extractor.extract_themes()
        print("\nTheme Areas:")
        theme_areas = self.theme_extractor.identify_theme_areas()
        for area in theme_areas:
            print(f"{area['theme']}: {area['description']}")

# Example usage

Some notable examples of AI applications in healthcare include:

2.  Google's DeepMind Health: An AI-powered platform that analyzes medical images and provides insights on diagnosis and treatment options.
3.  Microsoft's Health Bot: A chatbot that helps patients manage their health, answer medical questions, and access healthcare services.

Overall, AI has the potential to transform the healthcare industry by improving patient outcomes, reducing costs, and enhancing the overall quality of care.
"""

report = DraftReport(data)
report.generate_report()

```python
def analyze_trends(input_data):
    # Preprocessing: split input into sentences
    sentences = [sentence.strip() for sentence in input_data.split('. ')]

    # Identify key themes and sub-themes based on sentiment analysis
    themes = {
        'Improved Patient Care': [],
        'Streamlining Clinical Workflows': [],
        'Enhancing Patient Experience': [],
        'Reducing Medical Errors': [],
        'Population Health Management': [],
        'Increasing Efficiency': []
    }

    for sentence in sentences:
        # Identify key phrases and keywords
        if "AI" in sentence.lower():
            for theme, keywords in themes.items():
                match = any(keyword in sentence.lower() for keyword in keywords)
                if match:
                    themes[theme].append(sentence)

    # Extract additional themes from the text
    themes.update({
        'Advancements in Medical Imaging': ["X-rays", "MRIs"],
        'Personalized Medicine': ["genetic profiles", "medical history", "lifestyle factors"],
        'The Potential of AI in Cancer Treatment': ["IBM Watson for Oncology"]
    })

    # Print out the identified themes and their corresponding keywords
    print("Themes:")
    for theme, sentences in themes.items():
        print(f"{theme}:")
        for sentence in sentences:
            print(sentence)
        print()

# Test the function with the provided input data
input_data = """
Here are some key facts about the impact of Artificial Intelligence (AI) on healthcare:

1. **Improved Diagnosis**: AI algorithms can analyze medical images, such as X-rays and MRIs, to help doctors diagnose diseases more accurately and quickly.
2. **Personalized Medicine**: AI can help tailor treatment plans to individual patients based on their genetic profiles, medical history, and lifestyle factors.
3. **Predictive Analytics**: AI-powered predictive models can identify high-risk patients and predict outcomes for specific treatments, enabling early interventions and improved patient care.
4. **Streamlined Clinical workflows**: AI can automate routine administrative tasks, allowing healthcare professionals to focus on more complex and high-value tasks.
5. **Enhanced Patient Engagement**: AI-powered chatbots and virtual assistants can help patients manage their health, provide medication reminders, and answer medical questions.
6. **Reducing Medical Errors**: AI can analyze large amounts of data to identify potential errors in medical procedures and suggest improvements to reduce complications.
7. **Improved Population Health Management**: AI can analyze population-level data to identify trends and patterns, enabling healthcare systems to target interventions and improve health outcomes for specific populations.
8. **Increased Efficiency**: AI-powered robotics and automation can help reduce the workload of healthcare professionals, allowing them to focus on more complex tasks.

Some notable examples of AI applications in healthcare include:

1. **IBM Watson for Oncology**: An AI platform that uses natural language processing and machine learning to analyze large amounts of cancer data and provide personalized treatment recommendations.
2. **Google's DeepMind Health**: An AI-powered platform that analyzes medical images and provides insights on diagnosis and treatment options.
3. **Microsoft's Health Bot**: A chatbot that helps patients manage their health, answer medical questions, and access healthcare services.

Overall, AI has the potential to transform the healthcare industry by improving patient outcomes, reducing costs, and enhancing the overall quality of care.
"""

analyze_trends(input_data)

     | Risks:
     | # Assessing Risks: A Review of AI's Impact on Healthcare
     | 
     | ## Introduction
     | 
     | Artificial Intelligence (AI) has revolutionized various aspects of healthcare, transforming patient care, clinical workflows, and population health management. While AI offers numerous benefits, there are also potential risks and challenges that must be addressed. This report aims to assess the risks associated with AI in healthcare.
     | 
     | ## Risks Identified
     | 
     | 1. **Data Security**: The increasing reliance on AI-powered systems for healthcare data storage, analysis, and transmission raises concerns about data security. Unauthorized access or breaches could compromise patient confidentiality.
     | 2. **Bias in Decision-Making**: AI algorithms can perpetuate biases present in the training data, leading to uneven treatment outcomes and potentially discriminatory decisions.
     | 3. **Job Displacement**: Automation of routine administrative tasks may displace healthcare professionals, particularly those with high levels of automation-dependent jobs.
     | 4. **Dependence on Technology**: Over-reliance on AI-powered systems could lead to decreased critical thinking skills among healthcare professionals and reduced ability to perform complex procedures without technology support.
     | 5. **Lack of Transparency**: Complex AI decision-making processes can be difficult for non-experts to understand, making it challenging for patients to ask questions or seek clarification.
     | 6. **Cybersecurity Threats**: As AI systems become more interconnected, they may become vulnerable to cyber threats, which could compromise patient care and safety.
     | 7. **Regulatory Challenges**: The rapidly evolving landscape of AI in healthcare creates regulatory challenges, including ensuring that AI systems meet existing standards and guidelines.
     | 
     | ## Mitigation Strategies
     | 
     | To address these risks, the following mitigation strategies can be employed:
     | 
     | 1.  **Implement robust data security measures**, such as encryption, access controls, and regular security audits.
     | 2.  **Regularly audit and update training data** to prevent biases from becoming entrenched in AI decision-making processes.
     | 3.  **Invest in retraining programs** for healthcare professionals displaced by automation.
     | 4.  **Develop AI literacy and education programs** to enhance critical thinking skills and ensure transparency around AI decision-making.
     | 5.  **Enhance cybersecurity measures**, including regular software updates, patching, and incident response plans.
     | 6.  **Establish clear regulatory frameworks** to oversee the development and deployment of AI systems in healthcare.
     | 
     | ## Recommendations
     | 
     | To further assess the risks associated with AI in healthcare, we recommend:
     | 
     | 1.  **Conducting thorough risk assessments** for specific AI applications in healthcare.
     | 2.  **Developing guidelines and standards** for AI adoption in healthcare, including data security, transparency, and clinical validation.
     | 3.  **Investing in research and development** to improve the understanding of AI's impact on patient care and population health management.
     | 
     | By acknowledging these risks and implementing effective mitigation strategies, we can harness the benefits of AI while minimizing its negative consequences in the healthcare industry.
     | 
     | ## Conclusion
     | 
     | The integration of AI into healthcare presents numerous opportunities for improvement. However, it also raises concerns about data security, bias in decision-making, job displacement, dependence on technology, lack of transparency, cybersecurity threats, and regulatory challenges. By understanding these risks and implementing targeted mitigation strategies, we can ensure the safe and effective adoption of AI in healthcare.

```spl
-- Recipe 15: Automated Code Review
-- Multi-pass code review: security audit, performance check, style review, then synthesis.
-- Demonstrates tool-augmented analysis, multi-criteria evaluation, and structured output.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 code="$(cat myfile.py)"
--   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama code="$(cat main.go)" language="Go"
--   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama code="def foo(x): return eval(x)" language="Python"

WORKFLOW code_review
    INPUT: @code TEXT, @language TEXT
    OUTPUT: @review TEXT
DO
    -- Pass 1: Security audit
    GENERATE security_audit(@code, @language) INTO @security_findings

    -- Pass 2: Performance analysis
    GENERATE performance_review(@code, @language) INTO @perf_findings

    -- Pass 3: Code style and best practices
    GENERATE style_review(@code, @language) INTO @style_findings

    -- Pass 4: Bug detection
    GENERATE bug_detection(@code, @language) INTO @bug_findings

    -- Severity scoring for each category
    GENERATE severity_score(@security_findings) INTO @sec_score
    GENERATE severity_score(@perf_findings) INTO @perf_score
    GENERATE severity_score(@bug_findings) INTO @bug_score

    -- Synthesize all findings into a structured review
    GENERATE synthesize_review(
        @security_findings, @sec_score,
        @perf_findings, @perf_score,
        @style_findings,
        @bug_findings, @bug_score
    ) INTO @review

    -- Determine overall verdict
    EVALUATE @sec_score
        WHEN > 8 THEN
            COMMIT @review WITH status = 'critical_issues', verdict = 'block'
        WHEN > 5 THEN
            COMMIT @review WITH status = 'needs_fixes', verdict = 'request_changes'
        OTHERWISE
            COMMIT @review WITH status = 'approved', verdict = 'approve'
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
        -- Code too large — review in chunks
        GENERATE summarize_code(@code) INTO @summary
        GENERATE quick_review(@summary, @language) INTO @review
        COMMIT @review WITH status = 'partial_large_file'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @security_findings WITH status = 'security_only'
END
```

import ast
from typing import Union

def calculate_severity_score(security_risk: str) -> int:
    """
    Calculates the severity score of a security risk based on its type.
    
    Args:
        security_risk (str): The type of security risk ('Input Validation Vulnerability')
        
    Returns:
        int: The severity score of the security risk
    """
    # Define the severity scores for each security risk type
    severity_scores = {
        'Input Validation Vulnerability': 9,
        'SQL Injection': 8,
        'Cross-Site Scripting (XSS)': 7,
        'Buffer Overflow': 10,
        'Denial of Service (DoS)': 6
    }

    # Check if the security risk is in the severity scores dictionary
    if security_risk in severity_scores:
        # Return the corresponding severity score
        return severity_scores[security_risk]
    else:
        # Return a default severity score for unknown risks
        return 5


# Example use case:
risk_type = 'Input Validation Vulnerability'
severity_score = calculate_severity_score(risk_type)
print(f"The security risk '{risk_type}' has a severity score of {severity_score}.")


def safe_eval(x: str) -> Union[int, float]:
    """
    Evaluates the given expression and returns the result.
    
    Args:
        x (str): The input expression to evaluate.
        
    Returns:
        Union[int, float]: The result of the evaluated expression.
    """
    try:
        # Check if the input is a valid Python literal
        return ast.literal_eval(x)
    except (ValueError, TypeError):
        raise ValueError(f"Invalid input: {x}")


# Example usage:
expression = "10 + 5"
result = safe_eval(expression)
print(result)  # Output: 15

     | 
     | ### Input Validation Vulnerability
     | 
     | The provided code uses the built-in `eval()` function to execute user-supplied input. This is a major security risk because it allows an attacker to execute arbitrary code on the system.
     | 
     | **Why is this a problem?**
     | 
     | `eval()` can be used to execute malicious code, such as injecting SQL commands or downloading files from untrusted sources. It's considered a serious vulnerability in many programming languages and frameworks.
     | 
     | ### Recommendations
     | 
     | To fix this issue, we should replace `eval()` with a safer alternative that can handle user input without executing arbitrary code.
     | 
     | **Modified Code**
     | 
```python
def foo(x):
    try:
        return eval(x)
    except SyntaxError as e:
        # Handle syntax errors instead of exceptions
        print(f"Invalid syntax: {e}")
        return None
```
     | 
     | However, it's recommended to avoid using `eval()` altogether and use safer alternatives like:
     | 
     | 1. **AST parsing**: Parse the user input as an Abstract Syntax Tree (AST), which is a data structure representing the source code.
     | 2. **JSON or YAML parsing**: Use libraries like `json` or `yaml` to parse user-supplied input, which are designed for secure data exchange.
     | 
     | **Example with AST Parsing**
     | 
```python
import ast

def foo(x):
    try:
        return ast.literal_eval(x)
    except (ValueError, SyntaxError) as e:
        # Handle errors instead of exceptions
        print(f"Invalid syntax: {e}")
        return None
```

     | 
     | **Problem:**
     | 
```python
def foo(x):
    return eval(x)
```
     | 
     | In this function, the `eval()` function is used to evaluate the expression passed as an argument. However, if the input contains any Python syntax or expressions, it can lead to code injection attacks.
     | 
     | **Example Attack:**
     | 
     | Suppose we call `foo("1 + 2 * 3")`. The output would be `7`, but an attacker could potentially inject malicious code like this:
     | 
```python
foo("1; print('Hello, World!')")
```
     | 
     | This would execute the `print` statement and display "Hello, World!" on the console.
     | 
     | **Fix:**
     | 
     | To prevent this vulnerability, we should avoid using the built-in `eval()` function in favor of safer alternatives. Here's an updated version of the function that uses a simple arithmetic expression parser:
     | 
```python
def foo(x):
    try:
        return eval(x)
    except Exception as e:
        raise ValueError(f"Invalid input: {e}")
```
     | 
     | However, this approach is still not secure because it doesn't prevent arbitrary code execution.
     | 
     | A better solution would be to use a safer evaluation library like `numexpr` or `asteval`, which provide more control over the parsing and evaluation process:
     | 
```python
import numexpr as ne

def foo(x):
    return ne.evaluate(x)
```
     | 
     | Or, using `asteval`:
     | 
```python
from asteval import Interpreter

aeval = Interpreter()

def foo(x):
    aeval.symtable = {"x": 1}  # define a variable x with value 1
    try:
        return aeval(x)
    except Exception as e:
        raise ValueError(f"Invalid input: {e}")
```
     | 
     | **Conclusion:**
     | 
     | In summary, the `foo` function is vulnerable to "eval" injection attacks due to its use of the `eval()` function. To fix this issue, we should choose safer alternatives like `numexpr` or `asteval`, which provide more control over the parsing and evaluation process.

    """

        'Cross-Site Scripting (XSS)': 7,

        return 5

     |   @perf_score = import ast

     | def severity_score(code: str) -> int:

     |     This function evaluates the code quality and performance of the provided code.
     |     
     |     It returns an integer value representing the severity score, which ranges from 0 (low) to 10 (high).
     |     The score is based on the following factors:
     |     - Code readability
     |     - Use of `eval` or other evaluation methods
     |     - Presence of security vulnerabilities
     |     
     |     For this example, we'll use a simple scoring system:
     |     - Readability: 1-5 points (higher is better)
     |     - Evaluation method: 2-4 points (higher is better)
     |     - Security: 1-3 points (lower is better)
     |     
     |     The final score will be the sum of these values.
     |     """
     |     # Initialize the severity score to 0
     |     score = 0
     |     
     |     # Check if the code uses eval or other evaluation methods
     |     if "eval" in code:
     |         # If it does, deduct points for using a potentially insecure method
     |         score -= 2
     |         
     |     # Check if the code is readable
     |     if code.count("#") > 10:  # Assuming more than 10 comments means poor readability
     |         score -= 1
     |     
     |     # Check for security vulnerabilities (e.g., using untrusted input)
     |     if "input(" in code or "eval(input())" in code:
     |         score -= 3
     |     
     |     # Ensure the score is within the valid range
     |     score = max(0, min(score, 10))
     |     
     |     return score

```spl
-- Recipe 16: Reflection Agent
-- The agent solves a problem, then reflects on its own reasoning to catch errors.
-- If reflection finds issues, it corrects and re-reflects until confident.
-- Demonstrates meta-cognitive loops and self-assessment patterns.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 problem="What are the trade-offs of microservices vs monoliths?"
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama problem="Design a URL shortener system"
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama problem="Explain why bubble sort is O(n^2)"

WORKFLOW reflection_agent
    INPUT: @problem TEXT
    OUTPUT: @answer TEXT
DO
    @iteration := 0
    @max_reflections := 3
    @confidence := 0

    -- Initial attempt at solving the problem
    GENERATE solve(@problem) INTO @answer

    -- Reflection loop: assess and improve
    WHILE @iteration < @max_reflections DO
        -- Reflect: identify errors, gaps, or weak reasoning
        GENERATE reflect(@problem, @answer) INTO @reflection

        -- Score confidence in the answer
        GENERATE confidence_score(@answer, @reflection) INTO @confidence

        EVALUATE @confidence
            WHEN > 0.85 THEN
                COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'confident', confidence = @confidence, reflections = @iteration
            OTHERWISE
                -- Extract specific issues to fix
                GENERATE extract_issues(@reflection) INTO @issues

                -- Correct the answer based on identified issues
                GENERATE correct(@answer, @issues, @problem) INTO @answer

                @iteration := @iteration + 1
        END
    END

    -- If loop exhausted, commit best effort with final confidence
    COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'best_effort', confidence = @confidence, reflections = @iteration

EXCEPTION
    WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
        COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'max_reflections', confidence = @confidence
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        GENERATE solve(@problem) INTO @answer
        COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'restarted'
END
```
