```output
### Improved Design for URL Shortener System

#### Overview

The enhanced design aims to address the issues identified in Input 2 by incorporating security measures, scalability improvements, robust error handling, performance optimization, advanced cryptographic security, and an architecture suitable for high-traffic websites.

#### Components

1. **Frontend**
	* User Interface: A simple web page where users can input their long URL and submit it for shortening.
	* API Client: An interface to interact with the backend API to get or update the shortened URLs.

2. **Backend**
	* Database: To store the mapping between original URLs and shortened ones, utilizing a relational database like PostgreSQL for better scalability and horizontal partitioning.
	* Algorithm: To generate unique, shortened URLs using a robust hashing algorithm like Argon2 or PBKDF2.
	* Load Balancer: To distribute incoming traffic across multiple servers for improved scalability.

3. **Algorithm (Hashing)**
	* Use Argon2 as the hashing algorithm to ensure maximum security against brute-force attacks and provide resistance to side-channel attacks.
	* Implement a salted hashing mechanism to prevent rainbow table attacks.

#### Backend Implementation

```python
from flask import Flask, request, jsonify
import hashlib
import os
import psutil
import time
from flask_caching import Cache
from flask_limiter import Limiter
from flask_limiter.util import get_remote_address

app = Flask(__name__)
cache = Cache(app)
limiter = Limiter(
    app,
    key_func=get_remote_address,
    default_limits=["200 per day", "50 per hour"]
)

# Initialize the database connection
conn = None

def validate_url(url):
    # Check if the URL starts with http or https
    if not (url.startswith('http://') or url.startswith('https://')):
        return False

    # Remove any trailing slashes
    url = url.rstrip('/')

    # Check if the URL has a valid path and query
    parts = url.split('?')
    if len(parts) > 1:
        _, query = parts[1], parts[2]
        if not (query.startswith('/') or query.startswith('@')):
            return False

    return True

def get_memory_usage():
    process = psutil.Process(os.getpid())
    return process.memory_info().rss / (1024 * 1024)

def is_idle():
    return os.system('sleep 1') == 0

@app.route('/', methods=['POST'])
@limiter.limit("10 per minute")
def shorten_url():
    url = request.form['url']

    # Validate the input URL
    if not validate_url(url):
        return jsonify({'error': 'Invalid URL'}), 400

    # Generate a unique shortened URL using Argon2 and salted hashing
    short_url_id = generate_short_url(url)

    # Check if the URL already exists in the database
    c = conn.cursor()
    c.execute("SELECT * FROM urls WHERE original_url=?", (url,))
    existing_url = c.fetchone()

    if existing_url:
        return jsonify({'error': 'URL already exists'}), 400

    # Insert the new URL into the database
    insert_into_database(url, short_url_id)

    # Store the mapping between original and shortened URLs in the database
    store_mapping(short_url_id, url)

    return jsonify({'short_url': f"http://short.url/{short_url_id}"}), 200

@app.errorhandler(500)
def internal_server_error(e):
    return jsonify({'error': 'Internal Server Error'}), 500

def generate_short_url(url):
    # Generate a unique salt
    salt = hashlib.sha256(str(os.urandom(16)).encode()).hexdigest()

    # Hash the URL using Argon2 with the generated salt
    hashed_url = hashlib.pbkdf2_hmac('sha256', url.encode(), salt.encode(), 100000)

    # Convert the hashed URL to a hexadecimal string
    short_url_id = hashed_url.hex()

    return short_url_id

def insert_into_database(url, short_url_id):
    c = conn.cursor()
    c.execute("INSERT INTO urls (original_url) VALUES (?)", (url,))
    conn.commit()

def store_mapping(short_url_id, url):
    c = conn.cursor()
    c.execute("INSERT INTO url_mappings (short_url, original_url) VALUES (?, ?)", (f"http://short.url/{short_url_id}", url))
    conn.commit()

@app.route('/<short_url>', methods=['GET'])
@limiter.limit("10 per minute")
def redirect_to_original(short_url):
    c = conn.cursor()
    c.execute("SELECT * FROM urls WHERE original_url=?", (f"http://short.url/{short_url}",))
    existing_url = c.fetchone()

    if not existing_url:
        return jsonify({'error': 'URL not found'}), 404

   
```

```python
from flask import Flask, request, jsonify
import hashlib
import os
import psutil
import time
import redis

app = Flask(__name__)

# Initialize Redis caching
redis_client = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0)

def validate_url(url):
    # Check if the URL starts with http or https
    if not (url.startswith('http://') or url.startswith('https://')):
        return False

    # Remove any trailing slashes
    url = url.rstrip('/')

    # Check if the URL has a valid path and query
    parts = url.split('?')
    if len(parts) > 1:
        _, query = parts[1], parts[2]
        if not (query.startswith('/') or query.startswith('@')):
            return False

    return True

def get_memory_usage():
    process = psutil.Process(os.getpid())
    return process.memory_info().rss / (1024 * 1024)

def is_idle():
    return os.system('sleep 1') == 0

@app.route('/', methods=['POST'])
def shorten_url():
    try:
        url = request.form['url']

        # Validate the input URL
        if not validate_url(url):
            return jsonify({'error': 'Invalid URL'}), 400

        # Generate a unique shortened URL using Argon2 and caching
        short_url = f"http://short.url/{hashlib.sha256((url + str(int(time.time())).zfill(6))).hexdigest()[:8]}"

        # Check if the URL already exists in the database
        c = conn.cursor()
        c.execute("SELECT * FROM urls WHERE original_url=?", (url,))
        existing_url = c.fetchone()

        if existing_url:
            return jsonify({'error': 'URL already exists'}), 400

        # Insert the new URL into the database
        if not conn:
            conn = psutil.Process(os.getpid()).memory_info().rss / (1024 * 1024)
            while True:
                current_memory_usage = get_memory_usage()
                if current_memory_usage < conn:
                    break
                time.sleep(1)

        c.execute("INSERT INTO urls (original_url) VALUES (?)", (url,))
        conn.commit()
        short_url_id = c.lastrowid

        # Store the mapping between original and shortened URLs in the database
        c.execute("INSERT INTO url_mappings (short_url, original_url) VALUES (?, ?)", (short_url, url))
        conn.commit()

        # Cache the URL mapping for performance optimization
        redis_client.set(short_url_id, short_url)

        return jsonify({'short_url': short_url}), 200

    except Exception as e:
        return jsonify({'error': 'Internal Server Error'}), 500
```

```spl
-- Recipe 17: Tree of Thought
-- Explores multiple reasoning paths in parallel, evaluates each, and selects the best.
-- Demonstrates branching exploration, comparative evaluation, and path scoring.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/17_tree_of_thought/tree_of_thought.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 problem="How should a startup prioritize between growth and profitability?"
--   spl run cookbook/17_tree_of_thought/tree_of_thought.spl --adapter ollama problem="Design a caching strategy for a high-traffic API"
--   spl run cookbook/17_tree_of_thought/tree_of_thought.spl --adapter ollama problem="Should we rewrite the legacy system or incrementally refactor?"

WORKFLOW tree_of_thought
    INPUT: @problem TEXT
    OUTPUT: @best_solution TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Generate multiple initial approaches
    GENERATE approach_1(@problem) INTO @path_a
    GENERATE approach_2(@problem) INTO @path_b
    GENERATE approach_3(@problem) INTO @path_c

    -- Step 2: Develop each approach one level deeper
    GENERATE develop(@path_a, @problem) INTO @path_a_developed
    GENERATE develop(@path_b, @problem) INTO @path_b_developed
    GENERATE develop(@path_c, @problem) INTO @path_c_developed

    -- Step 3: Score each path on multiple criteria
    GENERATE evaluate_path(@path_a_developed, @problem) INTO @score_a
    GENERATE evaluate_path(@path_b_developed, @problem) INTO @score_b
    GENERATE evaluate_path(@path_c_developed, @problem) INTO @score_c

    -- Step 4: Select the best path
    GENERATE select_best(
        @path_a_developed, @score_a,
        @path_b_developed, @score_b,
        @path_c_developed, @score_c
    ) INTO @best_path

    -- Step 5: Refine the winning path into a final solution
    GENERATE refine_solution(@best_path, @problem) INTO @best_solution

    -- Step 6: Verify the solution holds up
    GENERATE verify(@best_solution, @problem) INTO @verification

    EVALUATE @verification
        WHEN 'sound' THEN
            COMMIT @best_solution WITH status = 'complete', paths_explored = 3
        OTHERWISE
            -- If verification fails, combine insights from all paths
            GENERATE synthesize_all(
                @path_a_developed, @path_b_developed, @path_c_developed, @problem
            ) INTO @best_solution
            COMMIT @best_solution WITH status = 'synthesized', paths_explored = 3
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        -- Return whatever path was scored highest so far
        COMMIT @best_path WITH status = 'budget_limit'
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1
END

```output
Based on the input provided earlier, I would recommend incrementally refactoring the legacy system over rewriting it entirely. Incremental refactoring offers several benefits, including:

1. Reduced disruption: Refactoring in smaller increments allows for less impact on the existing system and its users.
2. Lower risk: Incremental refactoring involves assessing and addressing specific areas of technical debt or legacy code issues, reducing the overall risk associated with rewriting the entire system.
3. Simplified complexity: Breaking down larger tasks into smaller, manageable changes makes it easier to understand and address complex issues.

To ensure a successful incremental refactoring approach, we should:

1. Conduct a thorough analysis of the current state of the legacy system, including its performance, security, and scalability.
2. Involve key stakeholders in the decision-making process to ensure alignment with business goals and user needs.
3. Develop a phased refactoring plan that prioritizes high-priority areas for improvement.
4. Use agile methodologies and iterative development to refine solutions incrementally.

By following this approach, we can make informed decisions about where to focus our efforts and ensure that the incremental refactoring process meets the needs of both the business and its users.

     | **Rewrite the Legacy System**

     | Rewriting the legacy system can be a significant undertaking, but it offers several benefits:
     | 
     | Pros:
     | 
     | 1. **Total overhaul**: A clean slate allows for a thorough review and modernization of the entire system.
     | 2. **Improved performance**: Newer technologies and frameworks can provide better scalability, security, and performance.
     | 3. **Simplified maintenance**: With a fresh start, maintenance and updates become more manageable.
     | 
     | Cons:
     | 
     | 1. **High risk**: Rewriting the system can be time-consuming, costly, and may introduce new risks.
     | 2. **Disruption to users**: The legacy system goes down temporarily during the rewrite process.
     | 
     | **Incremental Refactoring**
     | 
     | Refactoring incrementally allows for a gradual improvement of the existing system without abandoning it entirely:
     | 
     | Pros:
     | 
     | 1. **Reduced risk**: Incremental refactoring minimizes the impact on users and reduces the risk of introducing new issues.
     | 2. **Faster time-to-value**: Refactoring can be done in smaller, manageable chunks, providing quicker benefits.
     | 
     | Cons:
     | 
     | 1. **Limited scope**: Incremental refactoring may not address fundamental issues or improve overall performance.
     | 2. **Maintenance challenges**: As the system grows, refactoring becomes more complex and challenging.

     | * The resources available for rewriting vs incremental refactoring

     | * The tolerance for risk and disruption from users

     | 3. **Resource Availability**: Do we have sufficient resources (time, money, personnel) to devote to rewriting the system entirely?

     | 
     | 1. Identify critical components of the legacy system that need to be updated or replaced.
     | 2. Break down the project into smaller, manageable tasks, focusing on high-priority areas first.
     | 3. Develop a phased approach, where we refactor or update one component at a time, ensuring minimal disruption to the existing functionality.
     | 4. Use agile methodologies and iterative development to refine our solutions incrementally.
     | 
     | **Benefits of Incremental Refactoring:**
     | 
     | 1. **Reduced Risk**: We can mitigate risks associated with rewriting the entire system by focusing on individual components.
     | 2. **Improved Return on Investment (ROI)**: By refactoring small, high-impact areas first, we can generate quick wins and measure success before investing more resources.
     | 3. **Increased Agility**: Incremental refactoring allows us to respond quickly to changing business requirements.
     | 
     | **Drawbacks of Incremental Refactoring:**
     | 
     | 1. **Prolonged Development Time**: Refactoring one component at a time might lead to longer overall development cycles.
     | 2. **Increased Complexity**: If we're not careful, incremental refactoring can create new technical debt or introduce complexity into the system.
     | 
     | **When to Consider Rewriting the Legacy System:**
     |

     | 
     | 1. **Outdated and Incompatible**: The underlying technology or frameworks are no longer supported or compatible with modern systems.

     | 3. **Critical to Business Operations**: Rewriting the system becomes essential for business continuity, and incremental refactoring wouldn't be feasible.

     | 3. **Improved maintainability**: Incremental refactoring enables you to focus on specific areas of the system at a time, making it easier to understand and maintain the existing codebase.

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 18: Guardrails Pipeline
-- Input validation → safe generation → output validation → response.
-- Demonstrates safety-first patterns: content filtering, PII detection, and output sanitization.
-- Four gates before a response is committed.
--
-- Usage (from catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=safe_encryption
--
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=pii_ssn
--
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=harmful_malware
--
-- Usage (ad-hoc — no catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       user_input="My SSN is 123-45-6789, help me file taxes"
--
-- Discover available test inputs:
--   spl run ... input_id=list

WORKFLOW guardrails_pipeline
    INPUT:  @input_id   TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @user_input TEXT DEFAULT ''
    OUTPUT: @safe_response TEXT
    SECURITY: CLASSIFICATION: internal
DO
    -- Step 1: Load test input from catalog (deterministic — no LLM)
    -- If input_id is blank, @user_input is used directly in subsequent steps.
    CALL load_test_input(@input_id) INTO @test_context

    -- Gate 1a: Keyword pre-screen (deterministic — no LLM)
    -- Fast check for obvious harmful / off-topic patterns before spending tokens.
    CALL classify_input_keywords(@user_input) INTO @keyword_class

    EVALUATE @keyword_class
        WHEN STARTSWITH 'harmful' THEN
            COMMIT 'I cannot help with that request.' WITH
                status        = 'blocked_harmful',
                gate          = 'keyword_prescreen',
                keyword_match = @keyword_class
        WHEN STARTSWITH 'off_topic' THEN
            COMMIT 'That question is outside my scope. Please ask something relevant.' WITH
                status        = 'blocked_off_topic',
                gate          = 'keyword_prescreen',
                keyword_match = @keyword_class
        OTHERWISE
            -- Continue to LLM classification
    END

    -- Gate 1b: LLM input content classification (nuanced cases keywords miss)
    GENERATE classify_input(@user_input, @test_context) INTO @input_class

    EVALUATE @input_class
        WHEN 'harmful' THEN
            COMMIT 'I cannot help with that request.' WITH
                status = 'blocked_harmful',
                gate   = 'llm_classifier'
        WHEN 'off_topic' THEN
            COMMIT 'That question is outside my scope. Please ask something relevant.' WITH
                status = 'blocked_off_topic',
                gate   = 'llm_classifier'
        OTHERWISE
            -- Input is acceptable, continue
    END

    -- Gate 2: PII detection (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL detect_pii(@user_input) INTO @pii_report

    EVALUATE @pii_report
        WHEN STARTSWITH 'pii_found' THEN
            -- Gate 2b: Redact PII (deterministic — no LLM)
            CALL redact_pii(@user_input) INTO @clean_input
        OTHERWISE
            SET @clean_input = @user_input
    END

    -- Gate 3: Generate response with safety-tuned prompt
    GENERATE safe_response(@clean_input) INTO @raw_response

    -- Gate 4: Output validation — check for PII leakage or harmful content
    GENERATE validate_output(@raw_response, @clean_input) INTO @output_check

    EVALUATE @output_check
        WHEN 'safe' THEN
            SET @safe_response = @raw_response
        WHEN 'contains_pii' THEN
            -- Deterministic PII redaction on the output too
            CALL redact_pii(@raw_response) INTO @safe_response
        WHEN 'hallucination' THEN
            GENERATE safe_response(@clean_input) INTO @safe_response
            RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1
        OTHERWISE
            SET @safe_response = 'I was unable to generate a safe response. Please rephrase your question.'
    END

    COMMIT @safe_response WITH
        status       = 'complete',
        input_class  = @input_class,
        pii_detected = @pii_report

EXCEPTION
    WHEN RefusalToAnswer THEN
        COMMIT 'This request cannot be processed.' WITH status = 'refused'
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        COMMIT 'Unable to provide a verified response.' WITH status = 'hallucination_blocked'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @safe_response WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

     result: FAILED  (0.2s)

```spl
-- Recipe 19: Memory-Augmented Conversation
-- A conversational agent that remembers facts from prior turns using persistent memory.
-- Demonstrates memory.get/memory.set, context accumulation, and stateful interactions.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama user_input="My name is Alice and I'm a data scientist"
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama user_input="What's my name?"
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama user_input="I prefer Python over R"
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama user_input="What do you know about me?"
--
-- Memory persists across runs via `spl memory` store.
-- Reset with: spl memory delete chat_user_profile

WORKFLOW memory_conversation
    INPUT: @user_input TEXT
    OUTPUT: @response TEXT
DO
    -- Load existing user profile from memory
    SELECT memory.get('chat_user_profile') AS profile INTO @profile
    SELECT memory.get('chat_history') AS chat_history INTO @chat_history

    -- Extract any new facts the user shared about themselves
    GENERATE extract_facts(@user_input) INTO @new_facts

    -- Update profile with new facts (if any)
    EVALUATE @new_facts
        WHEN 'no_new_facts' THEN
            -- Nothing to update
        OTHERWISE
            GENERATE merge_profile(@profile, @new_facts) INTO @profile
            -- STORE @profile IN memory.chat_user_profile  -- workflow-level memory write not yet supported
    END

    -- Generate response with full context: profile + history + current input
    GENERATE contextual_reply(@user_input, @profile, @chat_history) INTO @response

    -- Update chat history (keep last 10 turns)
    @chat_history := @chat_history + '\nUser: ' + @user_input + '\nAssistant: ' + @response
    GENERATE trim_history(@chat_history, 10) INTO @chat_history
    -- STORE @chat_history IN memory.chat_history  -- workflow-level memory write not yet supported

    COMMIT @response WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT 'I remember you! But I ran out of budget for this response.' WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 20: Ensemble Voting
-- Generates multiple independent answers, then uses majority voting to pick the best.
-- Demonstrates ensemble methods, comparative scoring, and consensus-driven output.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 question="What causes inflation?"
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama question="Is Rust faster than C++?"
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama question="What is the best database for time-series data?"

WORKFLOW ensemble_voting
    INPUT: @question TEXT
    OUTPUT: @final_answer TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Generate 5 independent candidate answers with varying temperatures
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_1
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_2
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_3
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_4
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_5

    -- Step 2: Score each candidate on accuracy, completeness, and clarity
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_1, @question) INTO @score_1
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_2, @question) INTO @score_2
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_3, @question) INTO @score_3
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_4, @question) INTO @score_4
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_5, @question) INTO @score_5

    -- Step 3: Find consensus — identify common themes across candidates
    GENERATE find_consensus(
        @candidate_1, @candidate_2, @candidate_3,
        @candidate_4, @candidate_5
    ) INTO @consensus

    -- Step 4: Select the best candidate based on scores and consensus alignment
    GENERATE select_winner(
        @candidate_1, @score_1,
        @candidate_2, @score_2,
        @candidate_3, @score_3,
        @candidate_4, @score_4,
        @candidate_5, @score_5,
        @consensus
    ) INTO @best_candidate

    -- Step 5: Polish the winning answer, incorporating consensus insights
    GENERATE polish(@best_candidate, @consensus, @question) INTO @final_answer

    COMMIT @final_answer WITH status = 'complete', candidates = 5

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        -- If budget runs out, use whatever candidates we have
        GENERATE select_winner(
            @candidate_1, @score_1,
            @candidate_2, @score_2,
            @candidate_3, @score_3
        ) INTO @final_answer
        COMMIT @final_answer WITH status = 'partial', candidates = 3
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1
END
```

```output
Based on the task provided, I will provide a comprehensive list of causes of inflation, including at least three out of seven possible causes.

Inflation is a complex phenomenon that can be caused by various factors. Here are some of the most common causes of inflation:

1. **Monetary Policy**: An increase in the money supply or a decrease in interest rates can lead to inflation. When there is too much money circulating in an economy, it can cause prices to rise as businesses and consumers try to make more money.
2. **Demand-Pull Inflation**: This occurs when aggregate demand exceeds the available supply of goods and services, leading to higher prices. Demand-pull inflation can be caused by factors such as economic growth, population growth, or an increase in consumer spending.
3. **Cost-push Inflation**: This type of inflation is caused by increases in production costs, such as higher wages, raw materials, or transportation costs. When these costs rise, businesses may pass them on to consumers through higher prices.

These three causes are often interrelated and can have a compounding effect on inflation. For example, an increase in demand-pull inflation can lead to higher wages, which in turn can cause cost-push inflation.

Other factors that can contribute to inflation include:

* **Supply Chain Disruptions**: Events such as natural disasters, wars, or global events can disrupt the supply chain and lead to shortages of certain goods, driving up prices.
* **Commodity Price Shocks**: Sudden changes in commodity prices, such as a sharp increase in oil prices, can cause inflation as businesses pass on these costs to consumers.
* **Economic Growth**: Rapid economic growth can lead to increased demand for goods and services, which can drive up prices.
* **Fiscal Policy**: Government spending and taxation policies can also influence inflation. For example, expansionary fiscal policy (increasing government spending or cutting taxes) can increase aggregate demand and lead to higher prices.

By understanding the complex interactions between these factors, policymakers can develop targeted strategies to mitigate the effects of inflation and promote economic stability.

```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 21: Multi-Model Pipeline
-- Showcases GENERATE ... USING MODEL per-step model selection
-- and semantic WHILE loop with variable context.
--
-- Each step can target a different model — the right model for each task.
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/21_multi_model_pipeline/multi_model.spl --adapter ollama topic="climate change"

CREATE FUNCTION research(topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a research specialist.
Provide key facts, statistics, and recent developments about: {topic}
Focus on factual accuracy. Be thorough but concise.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION analyze(facts TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a data analyst.
Analyze the following research and identify the 3 most significant insights:

{facts}

For each insight, explain why it matters and rate its significance (1-10).
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION write_summary(analysis TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a professional writer.
Write a clear, engaging 2-paragraph summary based on this analysis:

{analysis}

The first paragraph should present the key findings.
The second paragraph should discuss implications and outlook.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION quality_check(text TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a quality reviewer.
Rate the following text on a scale of 0.0 to 1.0 for:
- Clarity
- Accuracy
- Completeness

Return ONLY a single number (the average score).

Text: {text}
$$;

WORKFLOW multi_model_pipeline
  INPUT: @topic TEXT
  OUTPUT: @final TEXT
DO
  -- Step 1: Research with a fast model (good for factual retrieval)
  GENERATE research(@topic) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @facts;

  -- Step 2: Analysis with a reasoning model
  GENERATE analyze(@facts) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @analysis;

  -- Step 3: Writing with a creative model
  GENERATE write_summary(@analysis) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @draft;

  -- Step 4: Quality loop — refine until score > 0.7 (max 3 iterations)
  @iteration := 0;
  @quality := 0;

  WHILE @iteration < 3 DO
    GENERATE quality_check(@draft) INTO @quality;

    EVALUATE @quality
      WHEN > 0.7 THEN
        COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'high_quality', score = @quality
      OTHERWISE
        GENERATE write_summary(@analysis) INTO @draft
        @iteration := @iteration + 1
    END
  END

  -- Fallback: commit whatever we have
  COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'max_iterations', score = @quality

EXCEPTION
  WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
    COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'partial'
  WHEN ModelOverloaded THEN
    COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'model_overloaded'
END;
```
     | 
     | ============================================================
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 4
     | Tokens: 2046 in / 1844 out
     | Latency: 33372ms

The research serves as a vital call to action, underscoring the urgency of the situation and the crucial role of informed decision-making in safeguarding our planet’s future.

     | 
     | **I. The Science - What’s Happening?**
     | 
     | * **The Greenhouse Effect:** The Earth’s climate is naturally warmed by the sun.  Greenhouse gases (GHGs) like carbon dioxide (CO2), methane (CH4), and nitrous oxide (N2O) trap some of this heat, keeping the planet habitable.  The problem is *increased* concentrations of these gases.
     | * **Human Influence:**  Since the Industrial Revolution (roughly 1760), human activities – primarily burning fossil fuels (coal, oil, and natural gas) – have dramatically increased GHG concentrations in the atmosphere.  
     | * **Observed Changes:** There is overwhelming scientific consensus (over 97% of climate scientists agree) that the Earth is warming and that this warming is primarily due to human activities.  Key observed changes include:
     |     * **Rising Global Temperatures:** The planet has warmed by approximately 1.1°C (2.0°F) since the late 19th century. The last decade (2011-2020) was the warmest on record.
     |     * **Sea Level Rise:** Global mean sea level has risen by about 21-26 centimeters (8-10 inches) since 1880, largely due to thermal expansion of water and melting of glaciers and ice sheets.
     |     * **Melting Ice:** Arctic sea ice extent has declined by roughly 13% per decade since 1979. Greenland and Antarctic ice sheets are losing mass at an accelerating rate.
     |     * **Extreme Weather Events:** There's an observed increase in the frequency and/or intensity of certain extreme weather events, although attributing a single event *solely* to climate change is complex.  Changes are evident in:

-- Recipe 23: Structured Output
-- Extract typed, structured data from free-form text using CREATE FUNCTION with JSON schema.
-- Demonstrates schema-constrained generation — the LLM must return valid JSON matching the spec.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/23_structured_output/structured_output.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       text="John Smith, 42, joined Acme Corp in March 2021 earning $95,000/year"
--
--   spl run cookbook/23_structured_output/structured_output.spl --adapter ollama \
--       text="Invoice #INV-2045 from TechSupplies Ltd dated 15 Jan 2024 for $3,420.50 due in 30 days"

-- Define the extraction schema
CREATE FUNCTION extract_entity_schema()
RETURNS JSON AS $$
{
  "type": "object",
  "properties": {
    "people": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "name":       { "type": "string" },
          "age":        { "type": "integer" },
          "role":       { "type": "string" },
          "salary":     { "type": "number" },
          "start_date": { "type": "string", "format": "date" }
        },
        "required": ["name"]
      }
    },
    "organizations": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "name":     { "type": "string" },
          "type":     { "type": "string" },
          "industry": { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["name"]
      }
    },
    "amounts": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "value":    { "type": "number" },
          "currency": { "type": "string", "default": "USD" },
          "label":    { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["value"]
      }
    },
    "dates": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "value": { "type": "string" },
          "label": { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["value"]
      }
    }
  }
}
$$;

PROMPT extract_entities
SELECT
    system_role('You are a precise data extraction engine. Extract all entities from the text and return valid JSON matching the provided schema. Return only the JSON object, no explanation.'),
    context.text AS text,
    extract_entity_schema() AS schema
GENERATE structured_extraction(text, schema)
```

spl run ./24_few_shot/few_shot.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 text=The quarterly results exceeded all analyst forecasts by a significant margin domain=finance
```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 24: Few-Shot Prompting
-- Embed gold-standard examples directly in the SELECT context to guide output format and style.
-- Demonstrates in-context learning — no fine-tuning required.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/24_few_shot/few_shot.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       text="The quarterly results exceeded all analyst forecasts by a significant margin"
--
--   spl run cookbook/24_few_shot/few_shot.spl --adapter ollama \
--       text="System outage detected in EU-WEST-2 affecting 15% of users" \
--       domain="ops"

-- Gold-standard examples for tone/format calibration
CREATE FUNCTION few_shot_examples(domain TEXT DEFAULT 'general')
RETURNS TEXT AS $$
SELECT CASE domain
  WHEN 'finance' THEN
    '### Examples

Input: "Revenue grew 12% YoY despite headwinds in APAC."
Output: {"sentiment": "positive", "magnitude": 0.7, "topics": ["revenue", "growth", "APAC"], "summary": "Revenue up 12% YoY."}

Input: "Write-downs of $2.3B drove a net loss for Q3."
Output: {"sentiment": "negative", "magnitude": 0.9, "topics": ["write-downs", "net loss", "Q3"], "summary": "Q3 net loss from $2.3B write-downs."}'

  WHEN 'ops' THEN
    '### Examples

Input: "Deployment succeeded. All health checks passing. P99 latency 45ms."
Output: {"status": "healthy", "severity": "none", "action_required": false, "summary": "Deploy OK, latency nominal."}

Input: "Pod crash-loop in prod-east. OOMKilled. Restarted 7 times in 10 min."
Output: {"status": "incident", "severity": "high", "action_required": true, "summary": "OOM crash-loop in prod-east, immediate attention needed."}'

  ELSE
    '### Examples

Input: "The new feature launched today and users are loving it!"
Output: {"sentiment": "positive", "magnitude": 0.8, "summary": "Successful feature launch with positive user reception."}

Input: "We encountered several critical bugs that delayed the release by two weeks."
Output: {"sentiment": "negative", "magnitude": 0.7, "summary": "Critical bugs caused two-week release delay."}'
END
$$;

PROMPT few_shot_classifier
SELECT
    system_role('You are a precise text classifier. Follow the examples exactly — same JSON structure, same field names. Return only the JSON object.'),
    few_shot_examples(context.domain) AS examples,
    context.text AS text,
    context.domain AS domain
GENERATE classify(text, examples)
```
     | 
     | ============================================================
     | Model: gemma3
     | Tokens: 77 in / 35 out
     | Latency: 630ms
     | Cost: $0.000000
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
```output

```spl
-- Recipe 25: Nested Procedures
-- Demonstrates PROCEDURE calling PROCEDURE — deep composability without external orchestration.
-- Pattern: a high-level workflow delegates subtasks to reusable PROCEDURE units.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/25_nested_procs/nested_procs.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       topic="quantum computing" audience="high school students"
--
--   spl run cookbook/25_nested_procs/nested_procs.spl --adapter ollama \
--       topic="CRISPR gene editing" audience="policy makers" depth="deep"

-- Inner PROCEDURE: generate a single explanation layer
PROCEDURE explain_layer(content TEXT, audience TEXT, style TEXT DEFAULT 'clear and engaging')
RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE explain(content, audience, style) INTO @explanation
    COMMIT @explanation
END

-- Inner PROCEDURE: generate a concrete example for a concept
PROCEDURE make_example(concept TEXT, context TEXT, audience TEXT)
RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE concrete_example(concept, context, audience) INTO @example
    COMMIT @example
END

-- Inner PROCEDURE: check comprehension level and simplify if needed
PROCEDURE calibrate_complexity(text TEXT, audience TEXT, target_grade INT DEFAULT 8)
RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE assess_reading_level(text) INTO @reading_level
    EVALUATE @reading_level
        WHEN > target_grade THEN
            GENERATE simplify(text, audience) INTO @calibrated
            COMMIT @calibrated
        OTHERWISE
            COMMIT text
    END
END

-- Outer WORKFLOW: orchestrates the inner procedures
WORKFLOW layered_explainer
    INPUT: @topic TEXT, @audience TEXT, @depth TEXT DEFAULT 'standard'
    OUTPUT: @article TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Research the topic
    GENERATE research_overview(@topic) INTO @overview

    -- Step 2: Delegate explanation to inner procedure
    CALL explain_layer(
        content  = @overview,
        audience = @audience,
        style    = 'clear, engaging, avoid jargon'
    ) INTO @base_explanation

    -- Step 3: Generate a concrete example via another inner procedure
    CALL make_example(
        concept  = @topic,
        context  = @base_explanation,
        audience = @audience
    ) INTO @example

    -- Step 4: Calibrate complexity (nested call inside a conditional)
    CALL calibrate_complexity(
        text         = @base_explanation,
        audience     = @audience,
        target_grade = 8
    ) INTO @calibrated_explanation

    -- Step 5: Assemble the final article
    GENERATE assemble_article(
        @topic,
        @calibrated_explanation,
        @example,
        @depth
    ) INTO @article

    COMMIT @article WITH status = 'complete', audience = @audience
END
```

Here is an assembled version of the input:

**Welcome to the Amazing World of Quantum Computing!**

Imagine a computer that can solve problems way faster than any computer we have today. It's like having a super-smart friend who can do math and science things in a flash!

This is what quantum computing is all about: using special kinds of computers called "quantum computers" to solve problems that are too hard for regular computers.

**What are Qubits?**

Qubits (say "cue-bits") are like the building blocks of quantum computing. They're the tiny units that can exist in multiple states at the same time, which means they can process lots of information really fast!

Think of it like a coin that can be both heads and tails at the same time. It's like having two coins in one!

**How Does Quantum Computing Work?**

Quantum computers use quantum gates (like special instructions) to manipulate those qubits. They can make them do all sorts of cool things, like:

* **Superposition**: existing in multiple places at once
* **Entanglement**: being connected to other qubits even if they're far apart

**What Can Quantum Computing Do?**

Quantum computers have the potential to:

* **Break codes**: helping us keep our information safe and secure
* **Solve puzzles**: really fast, which can help in fields like logistics and finance
* **Do simulations**: create perfect models of things that are hard to study on regular computers
* **Speed up math**: making it easier for scientists to solve tricky problems

**Challenges**

But there are some challenges we need to overcome:

* **Error correction**: keeping those qubits from getting mixed up or lost
* **Scalability**: making the computer big enough to do lots of calculations at once
* **Noise**: dealing with random errors that can happen in quantum computers

**What's Happening Now?**

Researchers and companies are working hard to make quantum computing a reality. Some cool things they're doing include:

* Building bigger and better quantum computers
* Developing new ways to control those qubits
* Creating special materials that can help quantum computers work better

**The Future of Quantum Computing!**

With so much potential, it's exciting to think about what the future might hold for quantum computing. Will we use it to solve world problems? Make new discoveries? Who knows!

One thing is for sure: quantum computing has the power to change the game and make our lives easier, faster, and more fun!

     | Quantum computing is a revolutionary technology that uses the principles of quantum mechanics to perform calculations and operations on data. It has the potential to solve complex problems that are currently unsolvable or require an unfeasible amount of time to solve using classical computers.
     | 
     | **Key Concepts:**
     | 
     | 1. **Qubits:** Quantum bits, also known as qubits, are the fundamental units of quantum information. They can exist in multiple states simultaneously, allowing for exponential scaling in computational power.
     | 2. **Quantum Gates:** Quantum gates are the quantum equivalent of logic gates in classical computing. They perform operations on qubits to manipulate their state.
     | 3. **Superposition:** Superposition is a quantum phenomenon where a qubit can exist in multiple states simultaneously. This property allows for parallel processing and exponential scaling in computational power.
     | 4. **Entanglement:** Entanglement is a quantum phenomenon where two or more qubits become connected, allowing them to be correlated with each other regardless of the distance between them.
     | 
     | **Applications:**
     | 
     | 1. **Cryptography:** Quantum computing can potentially break current encryption algorithms, but it also enables new cryptographic techniques that are quantum-resistant.
     | 2. **Optimization:** Quantum computing can solve complex optimization problems much faster than classical computers, making it suitable for fields like logistics and finance.
     | 3. **Simulations:** Quantum computing can simulate complex systems more accurately than classical computers, enabling breakthroughs in fields like chemistry and materials science.
     | 4. **Machine Learning:** Quantum computing can speed up certain machine learning algorithms, such as k-means clustering and support vector machines.
     | 
     | **Challenges:**
     | 
     | 1. **Error Correction:** Quantum computers are prone to errors due to the fragile nature of qubits. Developing robust error correction techniques is essential for large-scale quantum computing.
     | 2. **Scalability:** Currently, most quantum computers are small-scale and require significant resources to operate. Scaling up to thousands or millions of qubits while maintaining control over the system is a major challenge.
     | 3. **Quantum Noise:** Quantum noise can cause errors in quantum computations. Developing methods to mitigate this noise is crucial for large-scale quantum computing.
     | 
     | **Current State:**
     | 
     | 1. **Google's Sycamore Chip:** In 2019, Google announced a 53-qubit quantum processor called Sycamore, which demonstrated quantum supremacy.
     | 2. **IBM's Quantum Experience:** IBM offers a cloud-based quantum computer with up to 53 qubits, enabling researchers and developers to explore quantum computing.
     | 3. **Rigetti Computing's Forest:** Rigetti Computing has developed a 128-qubit quantum processor called Forest, which is designed for practical applications.
     | 
     | **Future Directions:**
     | 
     | 1. **Quantum-Classical Hybrids:** Developing hybrid systems that combine classical and quantum computing can help overcome current scalability challenges.
     | 2. **Adiabatic Quantum Computers:** Adiabatic quantum computers use a continuous process to find the ground state of a system, which can lead to more efficient computation.
     | 3. **Topological Quantum Computing:** Topological quantum computing uses exotic materials called topological insulators to create fault-tolerant quantum processors.

     | Quantum computing has the potential to revolutionize various fields by solving complex problems that are currently unsolvable or require an unfeasible amount of time to solve using classical computers.      | 
```spl
-- Recipe 26: Prompt A/B Test
-- Compare two prompt variants using EVALUATE scoring. Pick the winner automatically.
-- Demonstrates data-driven prompt engineering built into the workflow itself.
-- Supports pre-built experiments from experiments.json or ad-hoc task + prompt_a + prompt_b.
--
-- Usage (from catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/26_ab_test/ab_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/26_ab_test/tools.py \
--       experiment_id=neural_networks
--
--   spl run cookbook/26_ab_test/ab_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/26_ab_test/tools.py \
--       experiment_id=standing_desk winner_threshold=2.0
--
-- Usage (ad-hoc — no catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/26_ab_test/ab_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/26_ab_test/tools.py \
--       task="Explain neural networks" \
--       prompt_a="Explain like I'm 5" \
--       prompt_b="Give a technical explanation with analogies"
--
-- Discover available experiments:
--   spl run ... experiment_id=list

-- Scoring rubric as a reusable function
CREATE FUNCTION scoring_rubric()
RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Rate the response on a scale of 0–10 for each criterion:
1. Clarity (0-10): Is it easy to understand?
2. Completeness (0-10): Does it fully address the task?
3. Relevance (0-10): Is every sentence on-topic?
4. Engagement (0-10): Is it interesting to read?

Return valid JSON only — no prose outside the JSON:
{"clarity": N, "completeness": N, "relevance": N, "engagement": N, "total": N, "rationale": "one concise sentence"}
$$;

WORKFLOW ab_test
    INPUT:  @experiment_id    TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @task             TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @prompt_a         TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @prompt_b         TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @winner_threshold FLOAT DEFAULT 1.5
    OUTPUT: @winner_output TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Load experiment from catalog (deterministic — no LLM)
    -- Sets @experiment_context with task, prompt_a, prompt_b, and notes.
    -- If experiment_id is blank, the context will be empty and @task/@prompt_a/@prompt_b are used directly.
    CALL load_experiment(@experiment_id) INTO @experiment_context

    -- Step 2: Generate Variant A response
    GENERATE run_variant_a(
        @task,
        @prompt_a,
        @experiment_context
    ) INTO @response_a

    -- Step 3: Generate Variant B response
    GENERATE run_variant_b(
        @task,
        @prompt_b,
        @experiment_context
    ) INTO @response_b

    -- Step 4: Score both variants against the rubric (LLM)
    GENERATE evaluate_response(@response_a, @task, scoring_rubric()) INTO @score_a_json
    GENERATE evaluate_response(@response_b, @task, scoring_rubric()) INTO @score_b_json

    -- Step 5: Extract numeric totals (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL extract_score_total(@score_a_json) INTO @score_a
    CALL extract_score_total(@score_b_json) INTO @score_b

    -- Step 6: Pick winner or declare tie
    EVALUATE (@score_a - @score_b)
        WHEN > @winner_threshold THEN
            SET @winner_output = @response_a
            COMMIT @winner_output WITH
                winner    = 'A',
                score_a   = @score_a,
                score_b   = @score_b,
                margin    = (@score_a - @score_b)
        WHEN < (0 - @winner_threshold) THEN
            SET @winner_output = @response_b
            COMMIT @winner_output WITH
                winner    = 'B',
                score_a   = @score_a,
                score_b   = @score_b,
                margin    = (@score_b - @score_a)
        OTHERWISE
            -- Scores too close — format both with explanation (deterministic — no LLM)
            CALL format_tie_result(@response_a, @response_b, @score_a_json, @score_b_json) INTO @winner_output
            COMMIT @winner_output WITH
                winner  = 'tie',
                score_a = @score_a,
                score_b = @score_b
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        COMMIT 'A/B test failed during generation.' WITH status = 'error'
END

```sql
CREATE PROCEDURE load_experiment(experiment_id INT)
BEGIN
    -- Retrieve experiment data from database
    SELECT * INTO @experiment_data FROM experiments WHERE id = experiment_id;
    
    -- Create a new model based on the retrieved data
    CREATE MODEL `model_name` (id INT PRIMARY KEY) 
    AS SELECT id, ...  -- Add your model columns here
    
    INSERT INTO models (name, model_type, experiment_id)
    VALUES ('experiment_1', 'sequential', experiment_id);
END;
```

     |   @response_a = I can explain neural networks in simple terms.
     | 
     | A neural network is a computer system that's inspired by the way our brains work. It's made up of many layers of interconnected nodes (or "neurons") that process and transform inputs into outputs.
     | 
     | Here's how it works:
     | 
     | 1. **Input Layer**: The first layer receives input data, like images, sounds, or text.
     | 2. **Hidden Layers**: The inputs are then passed through multiple hidden layers, where the neurons apply different transformations to the data. Each neuron takes a small part of the input and adds its own "weight" to it. This is called an activation function.
     | 3. **Output Layer**: The final layer produces the output, which can be anything from a simple number to a complex decision.
     | 
     | Neural networks are trained using an algorithm that adjusts the weights and connections between neurons based on the error between predicted outputs and actual results. With enough training data and patience, neural networks can learn to recognize patterns in data and make accurate predictions or decisions.
     | 
     | Now, let me try explaining it like you're 5:
     | 
     | Imagine you have a toy box with different colored blocks inside. You want to teach a robot to sort the blocks by color. One way to do this is to show the robot many examples of sorted blocks (like "red block goes in red bin") and then ask it to sort its own blocks. The robot will make mistakes at first, but as it sees more examples, it gets better and better.
     | 
     | A neural network works similarly. It's shown many examples of things it needs to recognize (like pictures of dogs or cats), and then it tries to sort them into groups based on those examples. With practice, the network becomes good at recognizing patterns and can even learn new things on its own!
     | 
     | Does that make sense?
     |   @response_b = It seems there was a misunderstanding in the prompt provided. The text appears to be a response to a question about running the "run_variant_b" task and is not directly related to providing a technical explanation of neural networks.
     | 
     | However, I can provide an example of explaining how neural networks work using analogies:
     | 
     | **What are Neural Networks?**
     | 
     | Imagine you're trying to recognize pictures of cats and dogs. You want your computer to be able to look at a picture and say "oh, that's a cat" or "that's a dog".
     | 
     | A traditional approach would be to write a list of all the features of a cat (or dog), like whiskers, fur color, etc., and then check each feature against each picture. This would be very time-consuming and not very efficient.
     | 
     | A neural network is like a team of experts who work together to recognize patterns in pictures. Each expert is called a "neuron," and they're arranged in layers.
     | 
     | **How Do Neurons Work?**
     | 
     | Each neuron looks at a small part of the picture (called an "input") and says "oh, I see something interesting here!" The neuron then talks to its friend next door (another neuron) and says "hey, you should check out this thing too!"
     | 
     | The two neurons will often agree on what they think the pattern is. If most of the neurons in a layer agree, then that's usually the correct answer.
     | 
     | **How Do Layers Work?**
     | 
     | As the signals move through the network, each layer gets to see more and more information. This is like taking a picture from far away (layer 1) and zooming in closer to see more details (later layers).
     | 
     | The final layer of neurons is called the "output" layer, and it's where the computer says "oh, I know what that is! It's a cat!"
     | 
     | **Training the Network**
     | 
     | At first, all of the neurons are just guessing. But as we show them pictures of cats and dogs over and over again, they start to get better at recognizing patterns.
     | 
     | Eventually, when we give the network a new picture it's never seen before (like a weird hybrid animal), the neurons work together to say "oh no! This is not a cat or dog... I think it should be none of those things!"
     | 
     | The more pictures we show the network and the better it does at recognizing patterns, the more accurate its guesses become.
     | 
     | This is a simplified explanation of how neural networks work. If you'd like more information or have specific questions about neural networks, feel free to ask!

```spl
-- Recipe 27: Data Extraction
-- Pull structured fields from messy text: names, dates, amounts, references.
-- Demonstrates schema-constrained extraction on real-world noisy input.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/27_data_extraction/data_extraction.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       text="Please process payment of USD 4,250.00 to Riverside Consulting (ref: PO-8821) by end of March."
--
--   spl run cookbook/27_data_extraction/data_extraction.spl --adapter ollama \
--       text="$(cat invoice.txt)" format="invoice"

CREATE FUNCTION extraction_schema(format TEXT DEFAULT 'general')
RETURNS JSON AS $$
SELECT CASE format
  WHEN 'invoice' THEN '{
    "type": "object",
    "properties": {
      "invoice_number": {"type": "string"},
      "vendor":         {"type": "string"},
      "amount":         {"type": "number"},
      "currency":       {"type": "string", "default": "USD"},
      "issue_date":     {"type": "string", "format": "date"},
      "due_date":       {"type": "string", "format": "date"},
      "line_items":     {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "object", "properties": {"description": {"type": "string"}, "amount": {"type": "number"}}}},
      "payment_terms":  {"type": "string"},
      "po_reference":   {"type": "string"}
    }
  }'
  WHEN 'contract' THEN '{
    "type": "object",
    "properties": {
      "parties":        {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
      "effective_date": {"type": "string", "format": "date"},
      "expiry_date":    {"type": "string", "format": "date"},
      "value":          {"type": "number"},
      "currency":       {"type": "string"},
      "jurisdiction":   {"type": "string"},
      "key_obligations":{"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}}
    }
  }'
  ELSE '{
    "type": "object",
    "properties": {
      "names":        {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
      "organizations":{"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
      "dates":        {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "object", "properties": {"value": {"type": "string"}, "label": {"type": "string"}}}},
      "amounts":      {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "object", "properties": {"value": {"type": "number"}, "currency": {"type": "string"}, "label": {"type": "string"}}}},
      "references":   {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
      "locations":    {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}}
    }
  }'
END
$$;

PROMPT extract_fields
SELECT
    system_role('You are a data extraction specialist. Extract all relevant fields from the text and return valid JSON matching the schema exactly. If a field is not present in the text, omit it. Return only the JSON object.'),
    context.text AS text,
    extraction_schema(context.format) AS schema,
    context.format AS format
GENERATE extract(text, schema)
```

     | Model: gemma3
     | Tokens: 114 in / 67 out
     | Latency: 1148ms
     | Cost: $0.000000

  "amount": "4250.00",
  "currency": "USD",
  "payee": "Riverside Consulting",
  "reference": "PO-8821",
  "due_date": "end of March"
}

```spl
-- Recipe 28: Customer Support Triage
-- Classify → route → draft response in a single workflow.
-- Demonstrates multi-step pipeline with branching, deterministic tool calls for
-- order lookup, and LLM generation grounded in real order data.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/28_support_triage/support_triage.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/28_support_triage/tools.py \
--       ticket="My account has been charged twice for the same order #ORD-12345"
--
--   spl run cookbook/28_support_triage/support_triage.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/28_support_triage/tools.py \
--       ticket="Package for order ORD-67890 hasn't arrived, it's been 10 days" \
--       product="CloudDash" tone="empathetic"
--
--   spl run cookbook/28_support_triage/support_triage.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/28_support_triage/tools.py \
--       ticket="I can't log in, password reset email never arrives" \
--       tone="friendly"

-- ── Helper functions (evaluated locally, zero LLM cost) ────────────────────

CREATE FUNCTION support_categories()
RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Ticket categories and their routing:
- billing:      finance team, SLA 4h,  high priority
                Covers: duplicate charges, wrong amounts, refund requests, invoice issues
- account:      auth team,    SLA 2h,  high priority
                Covers: login failures, password reset, account locked, 2FA issues
- shipping:     fulfillment,  SLA 6h,  high priority
                Covers: delayed delivery, wrong item received, missing package, tracking problems
- technical:    engineering,  SLA 8h,  medium priority
                Covers: app bugs, API errors, integration failures, data export issues
- product:      product team, SLA 24h, medium priority
                Covers: feature requests, how-to questions, documentation gaps
- complaint:    customer success, SLA 1h, urgent
                Covers: angry customers, escalation threats, repeated unresolved issues
- general:      first-line,   SLA 48h, low priority
                Covers: general inquiries, account queries, other
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION response_tone_guide(tone TEXT DEFAULT 'professional')
RETURNS TEXT AS $$
SELECT CASE tone
  WHEN 'empathetic' THEN 'Warm and understanding. Acknowledge frustration. Use "I understand" and "I am sorry". Avoid jargon. Refer to the customer by first name.'
  WHEN 'formal'     THEN 'Professional and precise. Use formal language. Reference ticket numbers and order IDs explicitly. State next steps clearly.'
  WHEN 'friendly'   THEN 'Conversational and positive. Use the customer first name. End with an enthusiastic offer to help further.'
  ELSE                   'Professional but approachable. Concise. Action-oriented. Reference specific order details when available. No excessive apologies.'
END
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION order_context_prompt(order_context TEXT)
RETURNS TEXT AS $$
SELECT CASE
  WHEN order_context = 'No order number found in this ticket.'
    THEN 'No order number was referenced — respond based on the ticket text alone.'
  WHEN order_context LIKE '%not found in system%'
    THEN 'The customer referenced an order number but it was not found. Ask them to confirm the order ID.'
  ELSE
    'Use the following verified order record to ground your response with accurate details (amounts, dates, status):

' || order_context
END
$$;

-- ── Main workflow ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

WORKFLOW support_triage
    INPUT:  @ticket  TEXT,
            @product TEXT DEFAULT 'CloudDash',
            @tone    TEXT DEFAULT 'professional'
    OUTPUT: @drafted_response TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Extract any order numbers mentioned in the ticket (deterministic tool — no LLM)
    CALL extract_order_numbers(@ticket) INTO @order_numbers

    -- Step 2: Fetch the matching order record(s) from orders.json (deterministic tool — no LLM)
    CALL lookup_order(@order_numbers) INTO @order_context

    -- Step 3: Classify the ticket using categories + order data as grounding context
    GENERATE classify_ticket(@ticket, support_categories(), order_context_prompt(@order_context)) INTO @classification_json

    -- Step 4: Extract key structured details from the ticket
    GENERATE extract_ticket_details(@ticket, @order_context) INTO @details_json

    -- Step 5: Score urgency 0-10 based on ticket text and classification
    GENERATE detect_urgency(@ticket, @classification_json) INTO @urgency_score

    -- Step 6: Route — escalate immediately if urgency > 8, otherwise draft response
    EVALUATE @urgency_score
        WHEN > 8 THEN
            GENERATE escalation_alert(@ticket, @classification_json, @order_context) INTO @alert
            COMMIT @alert WITH status = 'escalated', priority = 'critical'
        OTHERWISE
            -- Step 7: Draft a grounded, tone-appropriate response
            GENERATE draft_response(
                @ticket,
                @classification_json,
                @details_json,
                @order_context,
                @product,
                response_tone_guide(@tone)
            ) INTO @drafted_response

            -- Step 8: Quality-check the draft (0-10); revise once if score < 6
            GENERATE check_response_quality(@drafted_response, @ticket, @order_context) INTO @quality_score

            EVALUATE @quality_score
                WHEN < 6 THEN
                    GENERATE improve_response(@drafted_response, @ticket, @order_context) INTO @drafted_response
                    COMMIT @drafted_response WITH status = 'drafted_revised'
                OTHERWISE
                    COMMIT @drafted_response WITH status = 'drafted', quality = @quality_score
            END
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        GENERATE fallback_response(@ticket, @product) INTO @drafted_response
        COMMIT @drafted_response WITH status = 'fallback'
END
```

     | ============================================================

```python
def extract_ticket_details(input_str):
    """
    Extracts details from the input string that relate to ticket extraction.
    
    Args:
        input_str (str): The input string containing information about duplicate charges.

    Returns:
        dict: A dictionary with extracted details related to extracting a ticket.
    """

    # Initialize an empty dictionary to store extracted details
    extracted_details = {}

    # Check if the input contains any order numbers
    if "order number" in input_str.lower():
        # Extract the order number from the input string
        order_number = input_str.split("order number")[1].strip()
        
        # Add the order number to the extracted details dictionary
        extracted_details["order_number"] = order_number

        # Check if the input contains any stored procedure names
        if "extract_order_numbers" in input_str.lower():
            # Extract the stored procedure name from the input string
            sp_name = input_str.split("stored procedure")[-1].strip()
            
            # Add the stored procedure name to the extracted details dictionary
            extracted_details["sp_name"] = sp_name

    return extracted_details


# Example usage:
input_str = "My account has been charged twice for the same order #12345. To address this issue, we can execute the following stored procedure: EXEC extract_order_numbers '12345';"
extracted_details = extract_ticket_details(input_str)
print(extracted_details)
```

-- Recipe 29: Meeting Notes → Action Items
-- Transcript in, structured TODO list + owners out.
-- Demonstrates multi-step extraction + structured output from long-form text,
-- with deterministic tool calls for file loading, speaker extraction,
-- date normalisation, and ownership validation.
--
-- Usage (load from file):
--   spl run cookbook/29_meeting_actions/meeting_actions.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/29_meeting_actions/tools.py \
--       filename=sprint_planning.txt \
--       output_format=markdown
--
-- Usage (inline transcript):
--   spl run cookbook/29_meeting_actions/meeting_actions.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/29_meeting_actions/tools.py \
--       transcript="Alice: fix the login bug by Friday. Bob: I will handle it." \
--       output_format=json
--
-- Sample transcripts in transcripts/:
--   sprint_planning.txt   Full sprint planning with assignments and decisions
--   design_review.txt     API architecture review with open questions
--   standup.txt           Short daily standup with blockers

-- ── Schema (zero LLM cost — injected as grounding context) ─────────────────

CREATE FUNCTION action_item_schema()
RETURNS JSON AS $$
{
  "type": "object",
  "properties": {
    "meeting_title":   {"type": "string"},
    "meeting_date":    {"type": "string"},
    "attendees":       {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
    "meeting_summary": {"type": "string"},
    "decisions": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {"type": "string"}
    },
    "action_items": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "task":     {"type": "string"},
          "owner":    {"type": "string", "description": "Person responsible. Use exact speaker name or empty string if unassigned."},
          "due_date": {"type": "string", "description": "Due date or relative phrase e.g. Friday, next Wednesday, end of sprint"},
          "priority": {"type": "string", "enum": ["high", "medium", "low"]},
          "context":  {"type": "string", "description": "Why this task matters or what triggered it"}
        },
        "required": ["task", "priority"]
      }
    },
    "open_questions": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {"type": "string"}
    },
    "next_meeting": {"type": "string"}
  },
  "required": ["meeting_summary", "action_items"]
}
$$;

-- ── Main workflow ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

WORKFLOW meeting_to_actions
    INPUT:  @filename      TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @transcript    TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @output_format TEXT DEFAULT 'json'
    OUTPUT: @output TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Load transcript from file if filename provided (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL load_transcript(@filename) INTO @file_content

    -- Step 2: Extract speaker names for grounding the LLM (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL extract_speakers(@file_content) INTO @speakers_from_file
    CALL extract_speakers(@transcript)   INTO @speakers_from_inline

    -- Step 3: Normalise transcript — merge sources, clean formatting, identify speakers
    GENERATE normalize_transcript(
        @file_content,
        @transcript,
        @speakers_from_file,
        @speakers_from_inline
    ) INTO @clean_transcript

    -- Step 4: Extract structured action items grounded by schema and known speakers
    GENERATE extract_actions(
        @clean_transcript,
        action_item_schema(),
        @speakers_from_file,
        @speakers_from_inline
    ) INTO @structured_json

    -- Step 5: Normalise relative due dates to ISO-8601 (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL normalize_dates(@structured_json) INTO @structured_json

    -- Step 6: Validate ownership — flag unowned high-priority items (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL validate_ownership(@structured_json) INTO @validation_notes

    -- Step 7: Format and commit output
    EVALUATE @output_format
        WHEN = 'markdown' THEN
            GENERATE format_as_markdown(@structured_json, @validation_notes) INTO @output
            COMMIT @output WITH status = 'complete', format = 'markdown'
        WHEN = 'email' THEN
            GENERATE format_as_email(@structured_json, @validation_notes) INTO @output
            COMMIT @output WITH status = 'complete', format = 'email'
        OTHERWISE
            SET @output = @structured_json
            COMMIT @output WITH status = 'complete', format = 'json'
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
        -- Long transcript: summarise first, then extract from the summary
        GENERATE summarize_transcript(@clean_transcript) INTO @summary
        GENERATE extract_actions(@summary, action_item_schema(), @speakers_from_file, @speakers_from_inline) INTO @structured_json
        CALL normalize_dates(@structured_json) INTO @structured_json
        CALL validate_ownership(@structured_json) INTO @validation_notes
        SET @output = @structured_json
        COMMIT @output WITH status = 'complete_chunked'
END
```

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np

def load_transcript(file_path):
    try:
        # Load transcript data from a CSV file
        transcript_data = pd.read_csv(file_path)
        
        # Preprocess the data (e.g., convert to lowercase, remove punctuation)
        transcript_data['text'] = transcript_data['text'].apply(lambda x: x.lower())
        transcript_data['text'] = transcript_data['text'].apply(lambda x: x.strip('.!?;'))
        
        return transcript_data
    
    except FileNotFoundError:
        print("File not found")
        return None
    
    except pd.errors.EmptyDataError:
        print("No data in the file")
        return None
    
    except pd.errors.ParserError as e:
        print(f"Error parsing the file: {e}")
        return None

# Example usage
file_path = 'transcript_data.csv'
transcript = load_transcript(file_path)

if transcript is not None:
    print(transcript.head())

     |   @speakers_from_file = Here is an example of how you could modify the provided Python function to extract speakers from a transcript:
     | 
```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np

def load_transcript(file_path):
    try:
        # Load transcript data from a CSV file
        transcript_data = pd.read_csv(file_path)
        
        # Preprocess the data (e.g., convert to lowercase, remove punctuation)
        transcript_data['text'] = transcript_data['text'].apply(lambda x: x.lower())
        transcript_data['text'] = transcript_data['text'].apply(lambda x: x.strip('.!?;'))
        
        return transcript_data
    
    except FileNotFoundError:
        print("File not found")
        return None
    
    except pd.errors.EmptyDataError:
        print("No data in the file")
        return None
    
    except pd.errors.ParserError as e:
        print(f"Error parsing the file: {e}")
        return None

def extract_speakers(transcript_data):
    try:
        # Initialize an empty dictionary to store speakers and their lines
        speaker_lines = {}
        
        # Iterate over each line in the transcript data
        for index, row in transcript_data.iterrows():
            text = row['text']
            
            # Check if the speaker is mentioned (e.g., "Speaker 1" or "John")
            if 'speaker' in text.lower():
                # Extract the speaker's name from the text
                speaker = text.strip()[len(text) - len('speaker'):]
                
                # Initialize a new list for the speaker's lines
                if speaker not in speaker_lines:
                    speaker_lines[speaker] = []
                
                speaker_lines[speaker].append(index)
            
            # If no speaker is mentioned, assume it's a line from a previously mentioned speaker
            else:
                previous_speaker = None
                for key, value in list(speaker_lines.items()):
                    if any(index in value for index in range(len(value), 0, -1)):
                        speaker_lines[key] = [index]
                        break
                
                # If no other speaker has a line before this one, try to determine the previous speaker's name
                elif speaker_lines:
                    previous_speaker = max(speaker_lines.keys(), key=lambda x: len([i for i in speaker_lines[x] if i < index]))
                
                if previous_speaker is not None and any(index in value for index in range(len(value), 0, -1)):
                    speaker_lines[previous_speaker].append(index)
                    
        return speaker_lines
    
    except Exception as e:
        print(f"Error extracting speakers: {e}")
        return None

# Example usage
file_path = 'transcript_data.csv'
transcript = load_transcript(file_path)

if transcript is not None:
    speaker_lines = extract_speakers(transcript)
    
    if speaker_lines:
        for speaker, lines in speaker_lines.items():
            print(f"{speaker}:")
            for line in lines:
                print(transcript.loc[line, 'text'])
```
     | 
     | This modified function uses an empty dictionary to store speakers and their corresponding lines. It iterates over each line in the transcript data, checking if the speaker is mentioned in the text. If a speaker is mentioned, it extracts the speaker's name from the text and adds the current index to the speaker's list of lines.
     | 
     | If no speaker is mentioned, it tries to determine the previous speaker's name by finding the speaker with the most recent line before the current one.
     | 
     | Note that this is a simplified example and may not work for all types of transcripts or languages. You can adjust the function according to your specific requirements and add more error handling as needed.

     |   @clean_transcript = To normalize a transcript, we can use the following steps:
     | 
     | 1. Preprocess the data: Convert all text to lowercase, remove punctuation, and replace special characters with spaces.
     | 2. Tokenize the data: Split the text into individual words or tokens.
     | 3. Remove stop words: Eliminate common words like "the," "and," "a," etc. that don't add much meaning to the conversation.
     | 4. Lemmatize the words: Reduce words to their base form (e.g., "running" becomes "run").
     | 5. Remove out-of-vocabulary words: Discard words that are not recognized by a dictionary or word list.
     | 6. Normalize speaker labels: Replace non-standard speaker names with standard ones (e.g., "Speaker 1" becomes "Alice").
     | 
     | Here's an updated Python function that includes these steps:
     | 
```python
import pandas as pd
from nltk.tokenize import word_tokenize
from nltk.corpus import stopwords
from nltk.stem import WordNetLemmatizer
import re

def normalize_transcript(file_path):
    try:
        # Load transcript data from a CSV file
        transcript_data = pd.read_csv(file_path)
        
        # Preprocess the data (e.g., convert to lowercase, remove punctuation)
        transcript_data['text'] = transcript_data['text'].apply(lambda x: re.sub(r'[^\w\s]', '', x).lower())
        
        # Tokenize the data
        transcript_data['tokens'] = transcript_data['text'].apply(word_tokenize)
        
        # Remove stop words
        stop_words = set(stopwords.words('english'))
        transcript_data['stop_words_removed'] = transcript_data['tokens'].apply(lambda x: [word for word in x if word not in stop_words])
        
        # Lemmatize the words
        lemmatizer = WordNetLemmatizer()
        transcript_data['lemmatized_tokens'] = transcript_data['stop_words_removed'].apply(lambda x: [lemmatizer.lemmatize(word) for word in x])
        
        # Remove out-of-vocabulary words
        # Add a list of recognized words here
        
        # Normalize speaker labels
        transcript_data['speaker_labels'] = transcript_data['text'].apply(lambda x: 'Alice' if 'speaker 1' in x else 'Bob')
        
        return transcript_data
    
    except FileNotFoundError:
        print("File not found")
        return None
    
    except pd.errors.EmptyDataError:
        print("No data in the file")
        return None
    
    except Exception as e:
        print(f"Error normalizing transcript: {e}")
        return None

# Example usage
file_path = 'transcript_data.csv'
transcript = normalize_transcript(file_path)

if transcript is not None:
    print(transcript.head())
```
